=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Another point-grab fest, where players are "islanders" trying to score the most points over 6 rounds.  Once again, theme takes a backseat to mechanics, but the mechanics work very well, and the game scales well from 2-4 players.  Like Feld's Trajan, there is no real punitive element here, rather a feeling of wanting to do more than you can, and potentially missing scoring opportunities if you don't/can't play optimally.

A new central dice mechanic is introduced, which is innovative as an action selection mechanism, but as with all things dice, it can also lead to frustration due to the rolls; it's so well implemented that the rolls can *mostly* be overcome through smart play.  

Essentially on your turn you roll 3 dice, and then in turn order place a die on an action with the caveat that only lower die can be placed if other die are already there.  You then execute the action immediately, and they are: 1)place/expand your huts, which can score points immediately, and/or at the end of the game, and which gives you a resource (which is needed for building) and this also clears room on your board, which is helpful, 2) grab a man/woman tile, which is then placed on your board (this is why you want the room cleared on the board), and these tiles allow for "extra actions" during another phase of each round, 3) build a building, which scores points and allows for a bonus fire action (a small but useful action that gives god cards or god offerings, and turn order advancement or shell currency), 4) make an offering to the gods, which scores points each round (think of it as an investment), gives a fire action, and potentially gives a god tile (a wild card essentially), and 5) convert die pips into: points and/or resources, and/or god cards, and/or offerings.

The next phase after die action selection is man/woman action, where you can activate the ability of 1 man and 1 woman.  The last phase is where turn order is evaluated (it is generally good to go first) and you score points for being further along the turn order track, temple scoring evaluation, jewelry purchasing (use shells and score points for purchasing), and finally task tile completion, which are conditions you need to meet to score points.  At the end of the game, there is significant final scoring as well, as you score for completing several different conditions (such as task-tile completion, jewelry purchases, board-clearing etc.), and you also score your hut placements if they are still "safe".  At any time during gameplay you can also play a god card + offering to improve/facilitate/rulebend your action(s).

There are alot of bits, so setup is a bit of a pain, the components are generally high quality, the weight is about medium, the scalability is good, and the gameplay is generally enjoyable.  Overall another successful Feld design.

Own the extra [boardgame=149712]god tiles[/boardgame].

RATING: 8
OK.  The term point salad is used a lot for Feld games, but I think this one takes the cake.  It's almost like he had two ideas for games and smashed them both together in this one.  I like it, but it is a bear to teach and is one of the few games where I have actually had people laugh at me during the rules explanation.  It gets a bit silly.

Like other good euros though, once enough people learn it it will be fun to play when you don't have to put in the time to teach it.  There isn't anything too challenging here, but there is a lot of it.

RATING: 7
Fairly complex game with a fun tribal theme. Many possible actions per turn and beautiful board. However, it amounts to rolling dice, using dice in one of many  ways to get points, repeating. I normally love dice, but depending on how you roll, it can be very limiting. Then, the game ends in a huge scoring phase. Upon replay, I like this game a lot more. I would not suggest this game to people who don't have experience with medium-weight games but once everyone grasps the game, it gets very exciting. In my last game, the final round was a super-stressful experience as everyone was desperate to squeeze out a few more points while others blocked them. 

If you have a regular gaming group of the same people, then it's a solid addition to your collection. If you play with many different people the way I do, then the somewhat-confusing rules and complicated iconography can make it difficult to get it to the table.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I've played and enjoyed many of Feld's titles. (I have a particular fondness for Luna). With this experience in mind, I found Bora Bora to be a surprisingly sprawling design. There is nothing streamlined here: just a huge, convoluted melting pot of different mechanics which don't really jar with each other, but don't sit entirely comfortably either. We have set-collection in a variety of forms, worker-placement, action-selection by dice placement, area-control, resource-management, turn-order manipulation. We've seen all of these elements before in tighter Feld games such as Castles of Burgundy, and In The Year of the Dragon: Bora Bora has nothing very innovative to offer. 

The game is an enjoyable, and difficult, puzzle to work on; primarily playing solitaire with occasional opportunities for blocking. Feld appears to be aiming to provide us with a game of great strategic depth, but the complexity here mainly comes from the dense opaqueness of the myriad mechanics. The gaudiness of the graphic design only makes it all harder to follow.

The evening I played Bora Bora, I also played Vikings and Keyflower. The streamlined, accessible rules, clever mechanics and depth of decision-making, in Vikings, showed Bora Bora up as the lumbering beast it is. Keyflower, with its twists and quirks on a tried-and-tested worker-placement formula, made the genre seem fresh, where Bora Bora feels a little old-hat.

This is definitely a game that a group could get their teeth stuck into, and enjoy exploring over a series of games, but it doesn't really offer anything that you couldn't find elsewhere in a more controlled package.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Bora Bora is a colorful game & the combination of cute huts, variety of men & women who lives in the huts, various type of fish, jewelries, men getting tattoed & women collecting shells & sending priests to temple really felt island-ish & so Bora Bora! Some of the best elements are the Helper action, the God cards & fire bonus. The gameplay is actually very easy, with 3 players, you just need to choose between 6 actions available, but the hard part comes from choosing the right dice, at the right action spot & the right timing for everything. Easily one of the best Feld game.

The game is actually easy, put dice on any of the 5 action tiles & you're done but you really need to plan your strategy well & pick your path to success, and that also depends on the task tiles you got. Man & Woman action in Phase B is very helpful towards achieving your personal goal. Gods are always there to help you too, if you have the offering to sacrifice. As always, you will not be able to do everything & you need to specialize.

Compared to Trajan & Burgundy, Bora Bora gameplay & boards are a bit messy & busy, not as smooth as the other 2 games. In summary, 6 rounds with 3 phases each round - roll dice & choose between 5/6/7 actions depends on player count (lower dice placement rule/blocking mechanism) then man+woman action (bonus) then status(turn order)-temple-jewelry-task update. What I like about the game is the various ways to get points, it rewards specialization, 6 rounds a bit shorter to do everything you want to do. It's colorful & have cute hut meeples. It has men & women you can recruit to help you advance your tribe & maybe complete a task or two. You can buy jewelry using shells collected by women which feels the most island-ish thing to do in the game. I need to buy new accessory box for the components & tokens.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Bora Bora is a solid Feld game, but hasn't passed Castles of Burgundy as my favorite.  It's an interesting Euro with lots of ways to score points and lots of options.  The use of the dice in the game is interesting and there are ways to mitigate the randomness, as is common in Feld games.  The main and player boards are a bit intimidating and busy, but once the game is learned, most everything you need to know to play can be found on them and they make sense.

So is this game different enough from Burgundy to warrant having both?  That depends on the person.  If you're a fan of Feld or Euros in general and have the money/shelf space to own lots of similar games, there are enough nuances between them to justify owning both.  If you want to keep your game collection trim and avoid having more than one game with similar mechanisms, you can get by having only one.  Bora Bora feels a bit more interactive than Burgundy, which is a little more on the multiplayer solitaire side.  There are more ways to directly interact with opponents in BB, though you can certainly still win without trying to screw your opponents.  I personally prefer Burgundy because of the tile-laying aspect of the player boards, but they're both very good games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Not my favorite Stefan Feld but certainly no slouch. Bora Bora is a very big very pretty "too clever for its own good" game with a lot of mechanisms crammed into one box. I think that it has some interesting things going on like a die based action drafting mechanism where you place a die on one of several Gods to trigger an associated action. Then, only dice that have lower numbers can be played on those spaces which means you can block or not block other drafts of that action. It leads to a sort of push your luck situation during play because you can want to use a given action multiple times but if you leave the god open by playing higher numbered dice on it to use the God's action than other players can use it too and possibly block you etc. the two biggest obstacles to me being able to get this to the table more are that set up time is significant due to there being so many components, and some of my game group doesn't like how much information in the game is public knowledge. They think this makes the game too front loaded and predictable. Personally, I can't keep track of all the information as it is a more than typical Feld point salad but I can understand the criticism. My personal hang up with the game is more minor in that I like so many of Feld's other games more that they out compete bora bora when I am deciding what to bring to game night. Still, I will probably be trying to bring this themeless sea horse out again soon as even a bad Feld is a good game IMO.

RATING: 7
What a deliciously intricate Feld-ian game.  Well, I have to admit, I think [b]Bora Bora[/b] reached the threshold of complexity I want in his games.  And maybe passed it a bit.  There are almost too many paths to victory here.  It's hard to get an idea if you are doing any better than your opponents.  It's brilliant, but a little tough.  It's coming up too close to the wrong side of the "work versus fun" scale.  Is this where Feld and I part ways?  That being said, the graphic design is a marvel.  So many details, so many pictograms, so many references...and all so clear!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.98
Abstract:
Bora Bora is about cultivating a community in the South Pacific. It is a heavy worker placement game by Stefan Feld using dice as worker. The pips on the dice define what actions a player can do.

Core actions:
a) Gain a male/female tile (for engine-building, extra actions, or fulfilling certain tasks)
b) Expand: place a hut on an adjacent area on the central board to gain resources and possible victory points (fish tiles)
c) Build: discard prepared resources to place a building tile onto the player board to gain victory points and additional boni
d) Temple: send a priest to the temple track (for a possible god tile and victory points at the end of a round)
e) Helper: trade pips for resources, cards, and actions
f) Fishing: score 2 victory points

Pros:
+ Dice used as workers (my favorite mechanism): pips on dice determine what actions are available
+ Each player has changing public personal tasks (not a sandbox game)
+ Limited number of actions each round lead to tough decision-making (every action counts)
+ Dice rolls and tasks requirements can be mitigated via god cards or god tiles: add flexibility by temporarily breaking the rules, mitigate randomness, use blocked actions, or achieve tasks
+ Mini expansion "orange god tiles" adds more mitigation for dice rolls (similar to the worker tiles in Castles of Burgundy)
+ Additional actions gained by male/female tiles (engine building)
+ The male/female tiles may lead to combination moves at the end of a round (planing ahead smartly)
+ Competition for the temple track: limited spots and the player with the most (highest valued) priests gains a very strong god tile
+ Huts used for gaining resources and for area control: the last player who places a hut in an area gains victory points for the adjacent fish tile at the end of the game
+ Plays well with all player counts. Available actions tiles depend on the player count. However, a 2-player game plays even more competitive because both players can focus on blocking each other
+ Artwork is bright and colorful, components are solid

Neutrals:
# More thematic than most of the other games by Stefan Feld
# High randomness in regard to the order of revealed jewelry/male/female/task tiles
# Tiles come in different order but there are no different elements or abilities per game (some tasks may not be revealed in a game)
# Competitive player interaction: temple track (kicking out priests or compete for god tile), the tattoo track (first player), bumping huts of other players (fish tile end game scoring), or block core actions with low valued dice -> players may only perform an already taken action if their pips are lower than the existing
# Good mix between tactical (available tiles, dice) and strategical elements (tasks, male/female engine building)
# No hidden information
# Scoring happens during the round, between rounds, and at the end of the game

Cons:
- Rules are okay, but could provide more examples and a better overview of the game in general. The game is not that hard to learn but the rules are not intuitive
- How to score fishing tiles at the end of the game should be read very carefully in the rules
- Hard to teach: a lot of mechanics and missing reference of cards and player aids (an overview of the round and possible actions would help a lot!!)
- Player boards look busy and overwhelming on first play (good iconography, but still I wish it would be separated to hide it when teaching the game)
- Fiddly: lots of tiles and pieces
- Rather long set-up time
- Playing time can be quite long if players are prone to paralysis analysis (no hidden information except for random male/female and tasks tiles each round)
- Low dice values are still useful, however, mostly used to block opponents for denial play (public personal tasks make it even more obvious what a player wants to achieve)

Thoughts:
A less intuitive but more thematic, complex, and competitive design from Stefan Feld. I think Bora Bora does not make a good job to introduce new players into the game, it is missing additional aids and overviews. What I like most is the dice mechanism similar to Castles of Burgundy. Bora Bora is a competitive game because players can block other players with low valued dice on purpose. It is not a mechanic that I usually enjoy, however, I still like the game because of the dice drafting in combination with the additional actions provided by the male/females tiles at the end of a round - players try to find the right combination of actions to achieve a task. The tasks are very cool and give players a goal to pursue. 

See also:
* Player aid: https://boardgamegeek.com/filepage/87956/player-aid-2-sided-a5-document
* Card reference: https://boardgamegeek.com/filepage/88626/bora-bora-task-tiles-menwomen-and-gods-reference

Similar games: 
* The Castles of Burgundy (using dice values to activate actions, less competitive)
* The Voyages of Marco Polo (dice used as workers, focus on resource management, point-to-point movement, and fulfilling contracts)
* Trajan (complexity-wise, number of mechanisms and tiles)

Player: 2-4 | Time: 60-120m

RATING: 7
This is not an easy eurogame. It is set on the island of Bora Bora, where a plethora of actions are available to gain victory points and ultimately win the game. The game somewhat catches up with the theme: you have to colonize the island, go fishing, build houses, have a family (with lots of members), go pearl fishing, etc. A nice touch to the game is the action selection, where dice will decide if you will be able or not to select the desired action. This brings a bit of randomness into this fairly calculable game. The artwork is nice, the game mechanics are good (one might say that it is a typical Stefan Feld game).

RATING: 6
I learned this one from someone who owned it but hadn't gotten a chance to play it yet, so it was 4 new players in a single game.  I think this game was somewhat interesting, and I'm impressed at Feld's ability to come up with Dice Action games.

The downside to this game is that I mixed my path through 4 or 5 different paths, and they all scored points.  There's a tiny amount of engine building in terms of the men and women you hire, but for the most part, any action you take leads to victory points, and there doesn't seem to be a reason for why things do what they do.

The game was varied, but forgettable.

RATING: 7
2-4(4)

I give Feld a lot of rope with his designs, and this is an enjoyable game, but his games feel like they are becoming increasingly bloated with different actions and different ways to score. This one has a bit more interest or tension for me than Trajan, which feels much less interactive, and forces you to sort of consider the whole board.

Nevertheless, it's one of those games where you set up the board for each round and then 80% of your setup gets wiped because nobody chose those tiles, which seems like a lazy way to add tension, decision-making, complexity.

RATING: 6.5
A typical (or more kindly, "classic") Feld with lots going on. A good action selection mechanism, lots of routes to VPs, although the theme gets a bit lost ("VP salad") it's a good example of this kind of game. Possible downside is that I find myself conscious of mistakes, being blocked, not getting the tiles you need, etc., rather than being pleased about what I am accomplishing.

This is the game, though, that made me tire of "pure" Euros. Really, what is the point of game after game of slightly different "points salad"? I'll play them occasionally, and enjoy them, but don't seek them out like I used to as I want more player interaction, and especially narrative.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Bora Bora is my favorite Feld title for several reasons. The luck of rolling the dice is compensated perfectly by the game mechanics, allowing a large variety of decision for every die roll. There are several paths to victory which can be combined into multiple viable strategies, making the game interesting even after several plays. The graphic art is amazing, colorful and appealing and it help blend the theme into the game play. Stripping the game down of all theme, it is still a good game for the 'thinkers' - those who take a lot of time to make the (almost) perfect decisions.

RATING: 4
I found Bora Bora annoying and unpleasant.  The theme felt mangled as the designer sliced and diced polynesian thematic elements, beyond common sense recognition, in order to fit them into his chosen mechanics, which were not particularly compelling for me to play with.  And I found the art to be childish, garish & (curiously) disrespectful.   

The overall effect for me was to transform what is widely considered an earthly paradise (Bora Bora) into something that felt like a long grinding series of cheap garish cartoon gaming problems.  Significantly, this cartoon depiction of Bora Bora was NOT a place I wanted to reside for a few hours, or for that matter, even a few minutes.

The overall interactivity of play felt low, and in our four player game, the downtime between player turns seemed like an interminable waste of time.  By and large, I cared very little what combinations the other players were accomplishing; yet, I had to sit through them and watch them in full boredom.  Essentially, I sat bored for five minutes between each turn. Rolling the dice each round was the very brief highlight of each round.

When a game mangles theme beyond common sense recognition, has low player interaction, features uninteresting mechanics, throws in seemingly arbitrary male/female roles, and presents uninteresting spatial challenges, for me, annoyance follows. Once was enough for this one.

RATING: 8
Certainly one of Feld's heavier games. In this one you once again use dice to carry out actions. But then the number on the dice make a difference for how well you can carry out those actions. And there are a lot of actions to take; Building huts, gathering resources, laying out shrines, appeasing the gods, hiring locals for work ,etc. There is a lot to do and you can even block other players out of actions by using the dice set at certain values. On top of that every round you will be trying to fulfill the demands of scoring tiles that become available each and every round. You may only have a certain number at the end of each round so you either score the tile or lose it. Then there is end of game scoring for set collection of different types of workers and jewelry that you buy over the course of the game using money collected each round. So do you stick with just scoring in the short term or sacrifice some of them to score in the long term. It's a thinky game but fun and looks extremely beautiful on the table even it does take up a lot of that table space.

RATING: 8.8
The initial feeling for this game is extremely positive. There's massive complexity, every choice is meaningful, artwork is gorgeous, and you can really develop strongly. The rules are the one negative aspect about the game, doing a subpar job explaining way too many moving parts, meaning that you'll spend a long time flailing about before the game starts to click.
The 4-player game feels a lot more cutthroat than the 3-player game (which I think I prefer), due to far more blocking.

Once you start to develop actual strategies for the game, you'll find yourself constantly and unintentionally blocked by other players. Being able to work around this is a key aspect of winning, but it can be pretty frustrating if you're not prepared for it, or if the randomness in the game just doesn't give you any break at all. The first round can sometimes be absolutely crucial for having a chance in the game (depending on available task and jewelry tiles), so that can really suck for inexperienced players who play together with more experienced ones.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
For those that enjoy point salads, Stephan Feld has managed to impress again; the game bombards you with useful information and has an abundance of interesting and rewarding tactical decisions. And like Castles of Burgundy, the game is a dice game that doesn't feel like a typical dice game; in other words, the dice mechanics seem to focus players more than limit/mitigate their decisions. That being said, there are two things that set this game apart from other Feldian point-salads. For one, the dice dictate the strength of actions but also limit players in an interesting worker-placement mechanic; thus, high-value dice yield higher results but are harder to play whereas low-value dice yield lower results but can block other players. And two, the game seems to have more player-interaction than other Feldian point-salads.

Overall, it is a very good design and I can see the rating of this game increasing with repeated plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Bora Bora.  Reading and absorbing rules took 90 minutes.  Lots of actions to choose from but these are quickly narrowed down as a result of die rolls as you can normally only chose an action by playing a lower numbered dice than those already played on the action.  A nice balancing effect between high and low rolled numbers.  But rolling 1,1 and 2 can still make progress difficult.  Trying to accomplish the target tile each season can be tricky.  Felt like having to feed the family in Agricola.

Stefan Brück usually includes a negative element in the Alea games, rats in Notre Dame for example.  But BB seemed pleasingly free of this type of negativity.  Fail to accomplish a task and it is no points, not minus points.

Also on the positive side, Alea at last seem to be reaching the production standards of the rest of the leading produces. Maybe Ravensburger have seen the light at last.  Card is now standard thickness.  Pieces are large enough - just.  But the game must include the most unnecessary box tray I have seen.  Pretty but pointless.  A case of trying a little too hard I think to catch up with the crowd.

BB is a bit of a slog.  Has some frustrations.  It is difficult to plan ahead for example when you are not sure which actions will be left to you.  But is engaging enough to deserve a few more outings which I look forward to.

RATING: 7
Szerintem magasan ez szerző legnehezebb játék eddig. Nekem a régebbi Feldek jobban tetszenek, mert valahogy egyedibbek. Az utóbbi időben inkább arról van szó, hogy minél több mechanimust egyre bonyolultabb masszává akar összegyúrni (ami egyébként alapvetően jól megy neki). Már a Trajan-nál is kicsit öncélúnak éreztem ezt, és itt is ez a helyzet. Pozitívum viszont, hogy a kockák miatt talán nincs nyerő (vagy túl erős) stratégia. Apropó kockák: a játék legnagyobb hibájának azt érzem, hogy közel 3 órás játékidő ellenére összesen 6x3 kockadobás alapozza meg a játékunkat. Ilyen kis dobásszámnál simán adódhat szélsőséges eredmény (pl. ha folyton 5-6-ost dobunk) és akkor hiába gyűjt az ember kismillió módosító istenséget. Épp ezért legalább annyira taktikai, mint stratégiai játék szerintem.
Egyébként szép, jól kidolgozott, ha nem tette volna magasra a mércét Feld, akkor lehet, hogy elájultam volna a játéktól. De játszani kellene még vele, akár mehet még feljebb is a pontszám.
---
Átpontoztam a Feldeket, csak 3 db 8 pontost hagytam: Burgund, Dragon, Notre Dame. A Burgundba nem nagyon tudok belekötni, de a másik kettő az, amivel legszívesebben játszom.

RATING: 8
Aki egy kicsit is jártas a játékok világában - és nem látná a dobozon az alkotó nevét - 10 percnyi játékot követően azonnal rávágná, hogy Stefan Feld jegyzi a tartalmat.

Szó szerint színpompás alkotás!
A lapkák, a kártyák, a játéktábla rajzolatai gyönyörűek, a színvilág meleg, a téma hangulatos (ki ne szeretné Bora Bora-t ?), a tartozékokat öröm kézbe venni.
Nem láttam még olyan játékot, ahol ennyi info lett volna feltüntetve a játékosok egyéni játéktábláján, mint itt...

Számomra ez a játék a "Trajan" kistestvére - okára nem tudnék magyarázatot adni, de a játék szerkezete, felépítése végig azt sugallta.
Talán azért, mert itt is sok-sok-sok lehetőség áll a rendelkezésünkre a pontszerzést illetően. 
Ötletes a dobókockák általi akcióválasztás mechanizmusa.

Hátránya, hogy a játékban rejlő lehetőségek leginkább 4 játékos esetén domborodhatnak ki igazán, két játékos esetén már nagyságrendeket veszíthet a játék az élvezeti értékéből. (ellentétben pl. a Burgunddal) Továbbá akad némi kiegyensúlyozatlanság néhány feladatkártya esetében - de ez a játék részét képez(het)i...

Nekem mindez "nyolc", annak ellenére, hogy biztosan lesz aki az első játékot követően biztosan rátalál majd a "nyerő stratégia"-ra ...

A játékidő hossza is teljesen elfogadható, továbbá a szabály sem annyira összetett, hogy csak gyakorlott játékosokkal lehessen játszani vele.

Ajánlott, de mindenképpen kipróbálandó, majd "élvezendő" kategória... 
... jaaaa, és a nagydobozos "alea" sorozat 15. tagja!

(Feld egyébként mesterien varázsolja bele a dobókockákat a játékaiba...!)

RATING: 8.9
At last, after so many years, alea shows mercy to Feld fans and displays a great production value!
Bora bora has exactly the theme of Hawaii, another great euro released almost the same time. Regarding complexity it is between burgen von burgund and trajan.
Regarding the gameplay, it is a great euro game. There is the endeavor-style mechanic, in the aspect that you control which type of actions will be available for you each round.
There is also the burgen von burgund element in which you get rewards for completing a set of pre-determined goals. (there are also random goals in the game)
The game is very good, and I put it among Feld's finest games. 
However, I particularly dislike the fact that only the last builder in each teriitory scores the points. I aim for a variant that reduces this chaos. My rating if we disregard this rule is 9.4

RATING: N/A
2 105.1
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Master Feld strikes back!

Bora Bora is a worker placement game, with dice used as workers. Higher numbers give access to more powerful options, but lower numbers are more easily playable (because every action space is not exclusive, and one can always place a dice where another was already put, as long as the new dice has a lower value).

As in many others games by the same author, there are many different sub-games (this time combined in a more coherent way than others) and there's a feeling of a "point salad", with every move resulting in some kind of point acquisition.

I'd say this is one of the better Feldian games. It is quite part of the canon, and Feld fans will love Bora Bora, while those who never got in touch with the clever teutonic dryness of the author's previous games won't change their mind...

A few words about scalability and components: the game plays wonderfully with four, very good with three, but with just two loses some tension.
The production value (graphic design, artwork, components) is way above the usual Alea average: Bora Bora is without doubt a gorgeous game, and the absolute best Alea title as far as the components are concerned.

RATING: 7
The turn order 'reset' is atypical for a Feld game.  Good use of dice.  I'm, not a huge fan of randomness, but Feld somehow won me over again.   

Update: The Man/woman tiles with the Build & fire action are overpowered.  We now play with a variant that these tiles get to build, but without the fire action.  

2nd Update: New variant is when revealing the Man/woman tiles each round, we reorder them based on the player mat from bottom to top.  That way the more powerful tiles, such as land movement, build & fire, etc. cost a higher die roll.  It's not entirely perfect for the point tiles should be higher up, but it works pretty well.  Playing with this variant, we no longer consider the build & fire tile to be overpowered, so we keep the fire action with it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Another point salad game from Stefan Feld that I enjoy very much. There is a style of gameplay in Bora Bora that make's it similar to other Feld designs; albeit with some new mechanics and ways to score.

 In and of itself, the game is very simplistic in it's rules and gameplay; the depth coming from the many paths and choices taken to achieve victory. At first, you are overwhelmed by the amount of choices available, but once the game gets going or you acquire some experience it all falls into place and you start enjoying the exploration of the different paths available for points.

 There is also more player interaction and conflict than in other Feld games, but just enough to still be considered in the standard Euro category.

If you enjoy other Feld games like Castles of Burgundy or Trajan, then this one is highly recommended.

RATING: 7
I am definitely starting to get the same feeling from Feld now.  I've been a supporter of Feld against a lot of those who say he's just putting together massive Euro-blender point games where it doesn't really matter what you do.  It does matter what you do, but there are too few differences between his games.  The use of dice in this game is magnificent.  Rolling high numbers is good (for the improved action) and bad (for the blocked actions, especially if you are later in turn order).  I enjoyed my one play of Bora Bora, but it didn't get me super excited to come back and play again.  Maybe after I'm away from it for a bit, I'll come back energized to try again.

It was better after the second play.  I'm still not amazed with this one, but it is definitely good.

RATING: 7
Ich hatte gestern einen sehr schönen Spieletag. Ich habe mich über die schönen Spielmechanismen in „Bora Bora“ gefreut. Dass ich nach nur einer Partie denke, dass das Spiel nicht richtig eingestellt ist, wird voraussichtlich an einem Denkfehler liegen, den mir gestern Abend aber fünf Leute nicht aufzeigen konnten. 
Ich hatte meine Partie gewonnen, indem ich nur auf den 3x4-Platz in der Mitte des Spielplans gegangen bin und die Priesterwertung dominiert habe. Das Ausbreiten auf der Insel und die Personen habe ich vernachlässigt. 
Aber wie komme ich dann an die vielen Rohstoffe für den 3x4-Platz (gewinnen kann ich nur, wenn ich frühzeitig (!) auf dem 3x4-Platz baue)? Antwort: Als 1:2-Tausch über die Aktionsmöglichkeit, die oben links auf dem Spielplan darstellt ist, oder über den Mann und die Frau, die jede Runde Rohstoffe bringen. (1 Mann und 1 Frau zu haben ist natürlich wichtig, sonst könnte man ja gar nicht von Phase B profitieren. Den Mann erhielt ich in Runde 2, die Frau in Runde 3.) Damit man den 3x4-Platz voll gebaut bekommt, braucht man einige weiße und blaue Karten (über Priester bekommen) oder lila Masken als Joker (über Priestermehrheit). Auf Tatoos verzichtet man ganz (man hat ja die Masken), nimmt sich, wenn man einen Priester platziert, also lieber Muscheln. 
Ich empfehle in der ersten Runde eine 6 in die Priesterleiste zu legen. Das schreckt die Gegner ab, und gegenüber unerfahrenen Spielern sollte man die Priestermehrheit unumkämpft behalten können. (In den Runden 5 und 6 legt man mit Priestern zu. Denn da gibt es 3 Punkte pro Priester.) 
Ich hatte in Runde 6 nur noch den 3x4-Platz voll zu bauen, mit den anderen beiden Arbeitern habe ich Würfelpunkte 1:1 in Siegpunkte eingetauscht. War primitiv, aber ich habe deutlich gewonnen. So wollte ich eigentlich nicht gewinnen. 
Die grünen Plättchen: Die dürfte ich nur in den Runden 4 bis 6 erfüllen können. Da aber relativ sicher. Wichtig ist, dass man langfristig plant, um am Ende viele von den bis zu drei Plättchen erfüllen zu können (mein Lieblingsmechnismus in dem Spiel). 
Ein Gegenspiel auf diese Strategie dürfte man nur mit vielen roten Karten bekommen, und die muss man erst einmal ziehen. Die Priesterwertung (für die lila Maske) will ICH ja auf jeden Fall gewinnen. 
Mein Fazit: Liebe Stefans, setzt, sollte ich Recht behalten (und das möchte ich gar nicht, weil ich das Spiel mag), zur zweiten Auflage die Punkte für die Fische herauf.

RATING: 10
Bora Bora is an excellent Euro that plays well with any number of players. It ties with Goa: A New Expedition for the first place spot in collection, and ties with Luna for "best Feld" spot.  Bora Bora offers top notch components, visually stunning board, multiple paths to victory, and high replayability in a medium-heavy Euro that craftly combines worker placement, dice-based action selection, and task tiles to deliver a solid gaming experience. There is a quite bit of randomeness in the game, and not just from the dice rolling, but with so many options available the luck factor is not that big of deal in this game. If anything could be improved in Bora Bora it would be the player interaction. I own other Feld games but have only played Macao and Bora Bora thus far. Macao for me is a 6, Bora Bora is a solid 10.  It is not the easiest of games to learn but once you get past the complex rules the reward is nothing short of gaming greatness. Watch this excellent walk through video and you will be playing Bora Bora in no time http://www.boardgamegeek.com/video/26681/bora-bora/bora-bora-walkthrough

RATING: 9
I have so much fun at any time I play this game. I am becoming Feld's fan :) I have to say - Bora Bora is complex, it is difficult and challenging and it has many rules. There is only 6 rounds divided for 3 stages: A (dice rolling & actions allowed by dices), B (bonus actions: men + women), C (Tatoo, Temple, Shells and Tasks).
I think the game is very pretty - it is a huge plus. It is not another dry, theme-less euro. I disagree this is an abstraction theme.
In this game it doesn't matter do You have small or big numbers when You roll dices, so if You roll small it is not end of the world (You have to plan longer and maybe use them to block an action or You can use one of 5 cool gods). 
Player board is wonderful - it has everything You need: space for all stuff You collect in the game & huge player aid on the left (contains rules shortcut so when You know You can do something but You don't remember how You don't have to search for the answer in the rulebook, just look at Your player board!) :) I love it!
The game is hard to find now and it is not cheap but it is worth every penny You spend. Solid stuff, wood (huts, monks etc), great main board and player boards, cardboard tokens like shells, gods offerings and much more. OMG!
Right now this game is one of my favorite complex eurogames.

Interaction in the game can be VERY mean and is very challenging & brain burning! The most difficult Feld's game I've played so far :) 

2 players - 80-90 minutes
3 players - ~120 minutes
4 players - >120 minutes

Easier Feld's game in similar theme -> "La Isla" :)

Orange God tiles recommended ;)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Not my favorite Feld. The design, as always, is clever, and the game is very challenging. I like that high rolls benefit you more, but can cripple you if someone takes a spot before you. Although this makes this game more interactive than his other games (which is a good thing), it also makes it more frustrating to play. 

The problem is that rolling all low numbers is still far worse than rolling all high numbers, and being able to block someone out of a spot is poor compensation for an entire turn where you can basically accomplish nothing. I think this is why I am not as crazy about this game compared to, say, the Castles of Burgundy, in which you usually have some useful thing to do with your dice. In Bora Bora, you find yourself continually screwed, seemingly on every other turn. There are plenty of other games which have a similar limiting/frustrating feel (Agricola comes to mind), but because in Bora Bora the pips on the dice are often the cause of your screwage, it can seem arbitrary and ultimately less satisfying.

RATING: 7
A little bit shocked to actually have enjoyed this! I've been more or less done with Feld for a couple years; Trajan was the back-breaker I think, his games are just so dry and mechanical and I don't enjoy the multivariate scoring problems the games present (Macao and Castles of Burgundy weren't good either). But the Bora Bora point salad is pretty fairly coherent, and the aesthetics of the games are easy to grok. It's also not totally abstract; the villagers with abilities and colonization options make some sense. It's a lot about choosing what you're *not* going to do, but the cool thing is that all the various avenues of action overlap in interesting ways. The die-roll action allocation system is also *much* better than any of his previous dice-rolling games (Burgundy, Macao) in terms of generating interesting decisions.

It's also fairly attractive, and being an alea game it seems very well-balanced.

There is still plenty not to like if you're not into Feld's highly structured, abstract, limited-player-interaction style, and the luck:AP potential ratio is not great, but nonetheless I enjoyed it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
If you've played other Feld games like Castles of Burgundy or Trajan, you know what you're getting into with this sort of game.  Oodles of ways to score points with no way to score them all, so you have to figure out the most efficient way to score those points, with many strategies being valid.

The unique mechanism here is that players each roll 3 dice, which they can apply to various actions.  The higher the die you place on an action, the more effective the action will be.  However, players place dice in turn order, and if other dice are already on an action, you can only take it by placing a lower valued die there.  So, rolling all 6's seems great until you get shut out of actions!  Of course, as is typical for Feld, he gives you some ways to manipulate things to still make it work if you prepared well enough, so you're not totally screwed unless you didn't plan ahead well enough.

Other than that main interesting mechanism, there is just a huge mish mash of ways to score points, which all somehow remain fairly intuitive and easy to remember despite their abundance and diversity.

I personally like this sort of thing, and I imagine if you've been playing games for a while you also know whether you'd like this or not before you even play it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

This is a very fun worker placement game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Great dice placement game with a lot of strategy despite it being a dice game (which I usually aren't too keen on)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
So . . . [thing=127060][/thing].

Bora Bora is a dice-placement semi-restrictive worker placement game; players can use spaces previously used, but only by placing lower-numbered dice.  Since the strength of each action is directly related to the number on the die placed, the game does a reasonable job of balancing the benefits of rolling high numbers (stronger actions) against the benefits of rolling low (broader choices of actions).  The game also provides "God" cards that allow players to break the dice-placement rules, but since you must spend actions obtaining those cards, you need to use them judiciously.

As it typical of Feld's designs, the game is overloaded with systems and subsystems, awash in options, and (potentially problematically) hyper-balanced.  There are a million things you can potentially do and everything is beneficial.  For example, there are six or so worker-placement spots where you can place a die.  A few of them allow you to select from a pool of tiles.  A few more allow you to spread your influence on a map in one of several directions.  One space allows you to spend your dice pips on the purchase of something like nine different items, while another space earns you one bonus from column A plus one bonus from column B, etc.  

As is also typical of Feld's designs, the thematic integration is blatantly terrible.  It's as if the man goes out of his way to mock the very concept of theme.  (As an example, the game comes with "fish" tiles and a "fishing" action space; but one has nothing to do with the other.  As another example, when players collect resources, they must place them in specific spots on a ceremonial grid and can only build buildings when two resources are orthogonally adjacent on the grid.)

The heart of the game, such as it is, is the management of one's huts (which occupy a shared, central board) and the accumulation of men and women (which provide certain resources and actions).  You need to place huts on the board to make space for the men and women you acquire.  You need to acquire men and women early to maximize your benefit from their free actions.  But you'd like to be the last person to place a hut in each region in order to score points.  So, like with everything in the game, you need to juggle cross-cutting incentives in order to score a few more points than you might have otherwise.  As you can tell from my description, it is not exactly a novel idea, but it done "well enough" here to give the game some legs. 

An additional interesting aspect of the game is the use of short-horizon objectives.  Each player begins the game with three random objectives.  At the end of each round, players can fulfill one objective for 6 points (or else discard an objective), and then players draft one new objective from a randomly stocked pool.  Thus, players are incentivized to work toward some particular goal every round, even when those goals are at odds with the player's broader strategy.  This mechanic reminded me a bit of Ora et Labora: mandatory use-it-or-lose-it point opportunities at designated intervals that may be unconnected to your larger path toward points. 

Speaking of "paths," players in our game were undecided whether the Bora Bora incentivizes specialization or diversity.  We all did a little of everything, because each action is beneficial in its own right and the actions themselves are sufficiently intertwined that it felt natural to combine them.  However, the end game scoring rewards certain heroic feats (like building 12 huts in a mere 18 actions) and I suspect that the skill on repeat plays will prove to be in focusing relentlessly on accomplishing one or two of those tasks without forsaking too many other tactical scoring opportunities.  

Okay, so let's cut to the chase: where's the vitriol?  Where is the foaming at the mouth, angry, rampaging, clobberin' time rant we've all come to know and love?  Sadly, not here.  Bora Bora is classic Feld, and in that regard I have little respect for the design.  This is not what an excellent board game looks like.  But that doesn't stop it from being fun.  I watch (and enjoy) terrible movies (I've seen Bring It On more times than I've every Best-Picture-Award-winner combined).  I watch (and enjoy) trash TV.  And while I typically try to listen to good music or read good books, I can't deny the catchy vapidity of the latest Taylor Swift ditty (spoiler alert: it's about a boy).  

Between the randomness of the dice rolling, the inadvertent (and advertent) screwage in the dice placement, and the ludicrously bad attempt to slap a theme on a bunch of abstract mechanics ("I'm going to spend two pips to tattoo my man for four Status points because I want to be first player when we buy jewelry") it's hard not to just sit back and enjoy the game for what it is.  It's not high art. It's a checkout-line romance novel and I have no doubt that it will sell more copies and entertain more people than whatever the board game equivalent of a Charles Bukowski anthology is.  

In terms of enjoyment, I put it in roughly the same category as Keyflower.  It is a poorly themed hodgepodge of familiar tropes: efficiency, synergy, interaction.  I would never play it with fewer than four for the same reason Tom Rosen likely thinks it is best with just two.  But it's good at what it does and what it does can be enjoyable if you stop taking yourself too seriously for a hot minute.  And, at least in that minute, it's worth keeping around. 





=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game plays out differently each time because of the task tiles you draw in the beginning and each round which, at least in my games, have shaped the strategy of the players. Sure, you can ignore them, but if you totally ignore them, you won’t win (at least that’s my experience as you can get 60 VPs for completing them). I think that the variety in the different tiles sets the game apart from many others as you have a goal before the game begins (and get more throughtout the game), and you try to play in accordance to this. The different setup adds a huge amount of variety and replayability too.

I have heard some people complaining about superior strategies; there is a thread about the man/woman building tile being overpowered, but I honestly don’t think it is. At all. I guess the biggest imbalance could be if one of the players bought all the 2 VP man or woman tiles and activated them each round with a green god – but the other players should definitely be able to prevent that player from getting the 3 similar tiles. The temple/priest track has been an important factor in my personal wins, but I definitely don’t think it’s overpowered; it’s only in the last two rounds that the priests earn some good VPs. Sure, the purple god tile is valuable, but it’s definitely not game-breaking.

Compared to Castles of Burgundy, which is another one of Feld’s good games, the luck of the dice is bigger in Bora Bora. This is because of the fact that higher rolls are almost always better (depending on the game situation etc.) and that it’s hard to manipulate the dice rolls (except for using a blue or a white god). I understand the criticism, but there is almost always something meaningful you can do with your dice. And as said, you can always use a blue god card to activate any of the actions or a white god card to make your crappy low die roll a 6. In my first few games I thought that the blue god was crucial, but I’ve come to appreciate the other god cards too as they can come in handy at certain times; the red god can net a big chunk of points if played at the right moment, the yellow god can help you completing a task which might be the key to score the end game 6 bonus VPs and, finally, the green god can really help by activating the man/woman tiles you want. Speaking of the man/woman tiles, they are very important in the game and I like the mechanic of the tiles and how you need to make room if you want to hire more. The tiles can be very strong, and players also face interesting decisions as to when to “activate” them for their bonus shells and especially tattoos for turn order! 

Another thing that I like about Bora Bora is that there is much more player interaction than in The Castles of Burgundy and many of Feld’s other games! Here, the interaction isn’t just limited to taking a tile another player wants – you can actually block the actions and thereby forcing people to forego their actions and have to take a measly 2 points instead of an action. Also, you can push other players’ priests out of the temple track and claim the land so that it is your huts and not their huts which score the fishing bonuses in the end - great!
Speaking of the end game bonuses, there really isn’t a whole lot of end-game bonus scoring. Sure, there are many things which give you 6 VPs in the end, but you’ll likely only be able to get two or three of these, and those 12-18 points isn’t a big deal in the end. Some of the end game bonuses are very hard to complete
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
7 2pl played:
Good game, usually willing to play.
But no need to own it myself:
* one central mechanic with added minigames = Trajan
* dice as central element, with strong ways to influence your rolls = Burgund
* many ways for scoring = Trajan, Burgund, Macao

I really dislike the random starting task draw; the 2 dark green tasks can (a) be very hard to fulfill and (b) dictate most of your play. To a lesser amount even the light green task can achieve this.

Bora Bora is an interesting game, but I prefer all of Macao, Burgund (with new tableaus) and Trajan to this one.

RATING: 4
So many moving parts. Maybe too many. My first impression is that this is likely an ultimately deeper game than Castles of Burgundy---but I kept thinking I'd rather be playing Castles of Burgundy. 

How is it that Reiner Knizia got so much flak for so-called 'theme-less-ness' and Feld just gets a free pass. Really? Why does he get a free pass? Because you have to be smart to play his games? It's such a forced mish-mash. My first thought---it needed a little more elegance and a little less peacocking. There are whole elements of the design (i.e the resource board) that seem completely unnecessary to the experience. 

Want to play again, but I was scratching my head MANY times during this game. Not because strategy isn't clear (it's not and I suspect people will very much like that aspect of the design), but I was scratching my head because I need a reason for things and an attempt at metaphor. He's letting the mechanisms determine the experience without much reason beyond "gameplay demands this". The design put itself in front of the experience. 

Prediction: no one will be playing this in 18 months. It's just another Seasons---really clever design that is not a rewarding enough experience for the effort. 

That said: The interaction design behind the icons made the game much easier to play then you'd expect given the lack of thoughtful coherence in the actual gameplay. Kudos to the user-interface team, impressive work. I may sound critical of the overall game, but I really do look forward to playing again. Not sure I like the color scheme and flourishes however.

RATING: 3
At first I assumed that this game would be akin to Castles of Burgundy and Trajan, but upon playing I discovered that this is not actually the case. Bora Bora is really much more a close relative of Macao than the previously mentioned puzzle games. The reason why I say this is because the interaction between players in Bora Bora is more pronounced; and there is actually potential for some nasty screwage, even if it is all mostly indirect. Or isn't it? You tell me how to call indirect interaction with yourself!

What struck me mostly about this game is that it is so [i]messy[/i]. If it were possible to visualise the mechanisms in a game, I'm sure we would all see many rivets, sharp metallic edges and uneven surfaces where a mallet was used to flatten the plate steel. Everything is tied together in a rather arbitrary way lacking subtlety and finesse. It works as far as I could tell, let that be clear, and I admit to being jealous that even this throwback from Stefan Feld's design table is probably already much better than whatever I can come up with... but it remains a mess. Add to this that the artwork is busy and annoying, and the result is a game I'm not particularly interested in anymore. Other Feld designs are already pressed for time at my table; I see no need to make it even harder on them.

RATING: N/A
WARNING!!! For die hard Feld Fans only. I can picture
myself trying to explain this game to new players. I can
already see the glazed looks in their eyes ,as they start
to dream about half naked tropical women dancing on rainbow coloured beaches , halfway through my explanation. 
  Man there was a lot to process. And after a 6 hour solo test run of a 4 player game, my brain fel(d)t
like it had just been through (a gamers equivalent)of an
LSD trip. My head was spinning with swirling psychedelic
colours and images of Gods and light dressed natives.

walking down the beach and get steamrolled by a runaway
train full of uncontrollable variables at any moment. Making this game an exercise in futility.
  I will leave this game unranked since there are so many other good games made by Feld. Hope his smaller
games Bruges/Rialto/Amerigo can restore my faith in this designer. Herr Feld !Please give players the illusion of control ,so that they may master their destinys.

RATING: 10
Not a fan of the rule book. All the info is there, I haven't seen a typo, but it is tough to see the forest because of the trees. 

An intro overview would have been nice, a little more theme added or explained? I mean are you building one big ceremonially building or 12 smaller ones. 

It makes the game easier to explain to others. Isn't that one purpose of a theme? An earlier reason of why you would or need to store extra resources outside the building area would have been nice. As it is they leave you dangling until you get thru the whole rule book. Hint, some of the task tiles need these resources too to complete them. Duh. If you spent the resources for a building you use up those resources as they go back to the supply.

The light colored task tiles are easier to complete then the dark green, is this mention? You soon discover this and is the reason you start with one light, the rest go into the box out of the game, maybe this was a clue I was suppose to pick up on aka going into the box, the other two are dark green tiles. 

Speaking of this light green task tile and two dark green tiles at setup they say you will place them on the player board later, I swear I never see when you are told to added them to the player's board. Why not just tell you to added them to the bottom of the board when you get them at setup?

Seems silly to complain but there is so much stuff and interlaced things in this game all the above are a distraction to figuring out and remembering the rules.

A short cheat info that the Phase B Man/Women phase mimics phase A, well, you getting more workers, duh, that the god cards/tile are basically die modifies.

That the purpose of taking the Helper action to use die points to move a hut is useful when you not want to populate a region because the last player who expanses into a region claims the fish/fishing ground at the end of the round. The Helper move seems silly and a waste until you realize it allows you to make room for a man/women tile and  plan for the end of the round fish points.

None of this info is a spoiler, you discover it painfully during your first play, stumbling thru the game, the rule book. It just would make it easier to grasp the rules if you knew why you would want to do this. Why is it in the game. 

After getting this knowledge the rules became easier to remember and the game just comes together. I didn't have this problem with the Castles of Burgundy, even FGG convoluted Space Hulk rule book was easier to understand and it is a pile of spaghetti.

Still, it is an excellent game and not that convoluted as it seems. If you have someone who can mock walk you thru a round this would be very helpful, the excellent WotC's video on how to play Lords of Waterdeep comes to mind. Maybe all publishers should consider this. 

Kudos, to the improved main board and parts over TCoB which has a cheap look and feel. Lots of kudos to the big, big improvement over the thickness of the player boards. 

This is a gorgeous game. I could have used some little larger wooden priest, but still, I appreciate that there is a lot of parts and table top real estate is at a premium.

Be warned there are a lot of pieces parts, setup can be a bit long. I take it some people didn't get the 7-8 ziplock bags in the box? Very nice it didn't come with an insert, spared me of having to mull over throwing it away.

RATING: 8
Feld-y Feld is Feld-y. Pretty much takes Feld's favorite mechanics and lays them on top of one another. The best combo is the role selection/dice allocation, which makes for some interesting decisions. God cards prevent total lockdowns, which is also nice.

The turn order track also works pretty well, as first is best until the very end, when you may prefer going last. 

On the downside, it's a bit tough to teach and feels very busy. Setup time is on the high side. Rounds can feel a bit samey, and there isn't a huge amount of difference between the various VP routes in many cases. Fish points at the end can be a little chaotic in multiplayer.

RATING: 5
I like the interactivity of the action blocking, but don't like that it's basically entirely unpredictable: even if you have a firm grasp of what everyone at the table wants to do and is capable of doing, until the dice are rolled, there's just no way to plan for blocking others or against being blocked (other than the blue god, which feels more like a "no, I'm NOT screwed" tacnuke than part of a crafted strategem).

Contrast with Macao, where you can divine longer-term interactions (blocking or otherwise) based on goods taken, ship position, neighborhoods selected, special powers, and the combinations of cubes queued up for future turns. Mind you, I don't think Bora Bora is trying to be like Macao and failing; I think it's simply a different type of game - just one I'm less fond of.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Solid design, but it could be a little more streamlined. It took us a long time to learn the rules, and the many options induced analysis paralysis, occasionally. I love the dice allocation though. It's very interactive, but not overly so.

Strategy thoughts:

First round goal: Try to have at least one man and one woman with useful actions by the end of the first round. The most useful actions are building and getting another man/woman (procreating?), I feel, because those actions free up your dice to be used for other actions. I got a man who let me build in the second round, and I used him every round for the remainder of the game. A person who does expanding (a pathfinder) or one that gets you resources or status is probably in the next tier of most useful actions, but you'll probably need to double them up with an identical person or a green god card to make them top-tier useful. A woman that gets you one seashell is, IMHO, a waste of space. There are far better ways to get seashells.

Second round goal: Build early and build often. Building early (in rounds 1 and 2) gets you the most points. You should build at least twice by the end of the second round. If you can manage to build three times by the end of the second round, I imagine you're doing very well indeed. If you can get a man who can build, it's more efficient to do it that way, I think, and that could potentially allow you to build twice per round in the early rounds. But, by the time you get to the fourth round, you'll need to double up on builders in order to use the building tokens with the higher die numbers or use a green card.

Third and fourth round goals: Get at least a couple priests into the temple. They'll pay dividends through the remainder of the game.

Complete as many tasks as you can. I guess this is obvious, huh? But this doubly applies to the end of the game. Make sure you have at least one yellow god card going into game-end scoring because that should allow you to score multiple tasks at the end.

Don't buy any jewelry that gives you only 1 point unless you're going for the game-end jewelry bonus. The 5-point ones are the minimum you should buy.

Read over the game-end scoring bonuses and keep them in mind. Make it a goal to score a couple of them. Some of them seem more difficult than others to achieve. There's some synergy between the maximum building and the completely-filling-in-your-ceremony-spaces game-end bonuses.

Sixth round goal: Expand into regions with lots of fishing points. This is probably the most efficient way to score points in the last round, especially if you have some red god cards or purple god tiles to effectively score the fishing twice. Try not to be first on the status track at the end of this round. In fact, if you can be last, none of the other players will be able to take away your fishing points (unless they move using a man/woman action in the next phase).

All the god cards are useful, but I feel the white cards are slightly more useful than the others. They let you maximize your helper actions and get you into the leftmost spot in the temple even when you roll low.

RATING: 8
Players are running a tribe on Bora Bora, trying to earn the most victory points.  Each player rolls three dice and takes turns allocating one of them to action tiles, and taking that action.  Dice can only be played on tiles that have a higher die result on them, and the value of the die generally indicates how much you can do with that die.  Actions include expand your chain of huts across the game board, earning resources.  Using those resources to build buildings.  You can hire men or woman into your tribe, which give you the potential for further bonus actions.  You can create priests to earn points and possibly a god tile (a wild god card - god cards grant special powers, as long as you have an offering to give them!).  The final action is the trader, where you can spend die pips on all sorts of things, from victory points to building materials, god cards and so on.  At the end of each turn, players get the chance to buy more victory points (jewelry), and complete tasks for further points.  

The game lasts six turns, with a further myriad of points granted at the end for maximizing expansion in certain areas of the game.  A very busy game, which Feld fans will lap up.  I liked it, and really admire the dice/action selection mechanic.  The rest of the game is a bit of a brain burner, and requires quite a bit of time to teach it to new players (who will likely be flogged during their first game).

RATING: 6
This game is a complete clusterfeld of mechanisms and point salad.  It works, to be sure, but it also feels like an experiment in how many mechanisms can be crammed into one game.  I think a bit of editorial distillation would have been a good idea.  It's like Castles of Burgundy and Trajan went on fertility drugs and had a baby.  A brightly colored, tile spewing, bloated baby.

I was definitely intrigued by Bora Bora game at first, but after several plays have come to the conclusion that I don't much care for it.  All the various mechanisms ultimately seem to take you to the same place at the same speed, which makes you wonder why the game had to be so complicated.  In my games, all the players ended up within a few points of each other, and that's with scores in the 150-200 range.  You are awash in choices, but as long as you are reasonably competent it doesn't seem that any of them are bad, so does it really matter what you choose to do? 

As usual for Feld games, the central feature is an action limitation mechanism. I've found it's a tenuous thing for me whether or not I enjoy the method of action limitation or if I find it frustrating.  My overall opinion of Feld games tends to rely upon this decision.  I very much enjoy the use of dice in Castles of Burgundy and I also really like the way the mancala works in Trajan (the Feld game most similar to Bora-Bora).  However, much like in Macao, I find the dice mechanism in Bora-Bora to be interesting in theory but frustrating in practice.  I'll stick with Trajan when I hunger for point salad!

I think I prefer Feld games that are a little more focused and streamlined - games like Notre Dame or Castles Of Burgundy.  This is more of a kitchen sink approach.

RATING: N/A
[b][ImpressionAfterMy2ndPlay][/b] This is Stefan Feld's 3rd game published in 2013 and also one of my most anticipated game of the year. 

The way I see it, this felt like Burgundy 2.0. Reason being, if you know how much 'point salad' is in Burgundy, then you get double the dose in Bora Bora. I don't know if that's a bad thing, but I think it can be overwhelmed to some as there are just too many ways for you to get VP. The only thing that's a similar between the two game is just the dice mechanic (in a way).

In Bora Bora, each player has 3 dice and takes turn playing one die one your turn, unlike Burgundy where you have 2 dice and need to use both on a single turn. I'll leave it to you to judge whether this one is prone to AP or not.

Half of your personal board also functions as game reference and it displays everything very well. The board is pretty and the pieces are just outstanding in terms of how they look and the quality. Speaking of quality, this is one of Alea's best produced game to date. The cardboard tokens (there's a lot of them!) are thick and easily punched from their frame. 

After playing many Feld games, I felt weird when Feld didn't choose to penalize a player when he/she can't fulfill one of their mandatory task at the end of a round (you simply discard it and get 0VP). Oh, I think it's worth mentioning that there's no penalty in this game. So is that a good or a bad thing?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The main idea of Bora Bora is an interesting one: you roll 3 dice and in turn order you place a die on one of the action selection tiles. You each do this 3 times and move on to the next phase. Higher numbers on actions give you a better action. Low numbers can potentially block other players from taking that action. The rule is you can place dice where others have placed but only if the number is lower than the lowest placed die on that action tile. There are things in the game that allow you to break this rule.

After the dice phase you have a action phase revolving around collected male and female tiles. You activate the special power of one male and one female tile you have on your board. If you collect sets of the same power amongst the same gender, you can boost that power. Sometimes diversification can be better though. To be able to get these male/female tiles you have to clear space on your personal board of huts in one of two ways. The best way is by placing your huts on the actual board. Board placement is a minor spatial element probably equivilant to Burgundy in complexity. That said, the spatial element is done collecively on the main board. There is also racing and blocking for the action that allows you to do board placement at all. 

After that you do a turn order/scoring/cleanup phase. There is some player interaction during this phase. During this phase players will complete and acquire tasks. These are basically in game objectives which will score you points. You can ignore them and play around with one of the other scoring systems, completely focus on them, or do a hybrid choice. 

The luck involved in the randomness of the initial tile placement and tile replacement during the game seems a little troublesome but its just an initial opinion. 

Then there are a lot of interlocking subsystems for end game scoring, in game scoring, and turn order in normal Feldian fashion. This is definitely a heavier game than Burgundy. It's still a point salad game however. I personally would rather play Troyes with 3 than Bora Bora. I would probably prefer to play Burgundy with 2 over Bora Bora. Bora Bora is a a good choice as a 4 player dice-rolling Euro.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Personally, I feel this game does not play well with two people. Specifically because (1) there isn't enough competition for goals, men and women tiles, actions, turn order, temple placement, etc., and (2) a huge flaw is that one player can wait for the first player to expand across the islands, then follow behind them and take over these spots, and by the end of the game one player can manage to steal all of the spots and get a HUGE bonus at the end of the game. Because a player cannot go back and reclaim a spot on an island, once it is stolen they lose the victory points and can no longer compete in this area. 

In a three and four-player game both of these negative points disappear and the game is quite enjoyable. For 2 players, I would rate this as low as a 6, for 3-4 players I would rate it an 8.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I've yet to find a Feld game I really *like*.  Bora Bora is the third game of his that I have played (Bruges and castles of Burgundy being the other two).  Dice placement games are also not my favorite mechanism; Alien Frontiers also fell flat with me.

That said, in Bora Bora you can't do everything.  The game is up front about that.  You'll get 6 rounds, and throw three dice each round.  That means, in total, you will have 18 actions.  You get additional points if you can complete one strategy, and work on a second (picking up easy points elsewhere, if possible).

My issue is that the gameplay is not interesting, and because everything is so scarce, you completely neglect certain aspects.  There's no tension, because you *just don't care*.  Someone claiming the temples, and you aren't focusing on that?  Meh.  Someone took a Woman, sure, fine.

Oh, and to make the game even less tense, Feld has added these god cards you can get to break rules.  So, the mechanism that would drive tension (dice placement) is alleviated.

Uninspired game play that lacks any tension.  Completely forgettable experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
My personal favorite game by Feld, and quite possibly my favorite game of all time.  It's an absolute masterpiece from start to finish.  The design is flawless, and the intertwined mechanics have a strong connection to each other.  The game also has a nice "progression arc" to it.

There is a good amount of interaction, and there are plenty of opportunities to gain huge point swings.  Like Castles of Burgundy, the use of dice is independent for each player (compared to Macao's dice that have the same impact across all players).  Feld's biggest talent is his ability to invent unique (yet VERY clever) mechanisms into the core of his designs.  Feld was *THE* guy who made me a fan of dice again!  He was *THE* guy who created initiative track systems that are meaningful and well-integrated.  He was also *THE* guy who twisted the ancient "Mancala" mechanic into blissful brain burning frustrations.  I can go on and on.  Clearly the man's a genius and Bora Bora is no exception.

One aspect I've noticed with Bora Bora (same goes with Castles) is how the last round is often "pre-scripted" by the players.  In other words, there isn't much dodging around the tactics & strategies during the final turn.  This is typical because players have carefully planned their moves 1-2 turns ahead for the endgame, and are often committed on their path.

The last round of Castles has the same effect.  But Castles can sometimes feel a tiny bit "anti-climactic" (compared to the brilliance before it).  Still a superb game though!  Yet... for some reason I do [u]not[/u] get any such feelings with Bora Bora despite the (sometimes) scripted ending.

A++  Feld.  My hat's off to you, and I can't wait for your next masterpiece!

RATING: 8
Wasn't sure through the first round if I was going to like this one as it really is another example of Feld's "point salad" approach to design - and this one revels in it! That being said, the core idea of higher rolls giving increased payouts but smaller chances of being able to play them is brilliant and Feld once again surprises me with a design that I have not yet seen and wished I invented myself! Then he built an (at-first) overwhelming number of ways to score points and bonus actions. It's a dizzying mess of symbols and graphics! Once you get through the first round however, it starts to make sense and by the end of the second round this game really zings!  The design choices actually all work together quite well and make this a much more heavier Euro than I was expecting.  But it all makes sense!  The goals create a great amount of tension in the game.  Other than it being somewhat of a mess to teach to newcomers, this is a great game!  I wish it could have been simmered a little bit more without risking the loss of any of its impact, but maybe that wasn't possible.  Overall, quite enjoyable!

2016 - August

RATING: 8
5.9.17 

Probably my second favorite Feld after Luna but it's a somewhat distant second. Rialto might surpass Bora Bora in terms of being slightly less fiddly with lower rules overhead but still enjoyable for the decisions possible.

PROS
-Lots of player interaction.
-Lots of varying strategies.
-Gameplay is going to vary a lot from game to game.
-Busy but still pretty to look at.

CONS
-BB is a number of mini-games that feel more connected than Trajan but has a bit of that kitchen-sink type design.
-Fiiiiiiiddly to the extreme: an absurd number of chits, tokens, pieces, and cards.
-Numerous niggling rules questions but not a deal breaker.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Bora Bora is directly competing with Castles of Burgundy for my favorite game of all time. It's an amazing game, but there are few things going against it as well.

Some of the good things (this list is much longer in my head): 
- Dice mechanism is a new twist on worker placement. It's original and familiar at the same time. It is essentially the same as in any classic WP, such as Agricola - but your workers are "weighted" here. In Agricola, workers a strictly blocking (as if they were all dice value "1"). In Bora Bora, the higher the dice value, the stronger the worker power, but it's ability to block and to not be blocked decreases. Simple and genius. 
- I love how tasks are implemented here. You sort of feel that you are filling 3 glasses at once, trying to make sure you have at least one glass full by the end of a round, at which point you replace it with a new glass, and by the end of the game you need to fill 9 glasses. It's a very pleasant balancing act. 
- Island minigame actually reminds me of Burgundy, yet it is different enough. While the map itself is static, the fishes are laid out randomly each game, making the island setup different and presenting a new puzzle every time. 

Not so good:
- I love tropical theme. In Bora Bora however, it doesn't work. Not necessarily because of the weak thematic connection of mechanics, which is expected of Feld, but because tropical theme sets expectation of something more relaxing and lighthearted, while here we get nothing but a heavy euro which requires you to concentrate and work.
- The game feels slightly "overwritten". I'm afraid to teach it anybody but the most seasoned gamers. That severely limits my options to actually get this game out..
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Fun theme which works well, but there are a lot going on the first time you play it. Could be annoying with the dice in the long run, but I think I can manage it here.

RATING: 7
A good game although with a limited replayability.

I think this game is worth checking out for anyone who have never seen it and likes something different than your usual worker placement.

I like the help action, it is quite original the way you use your "worker" you use the pips on the die for the number of actions you can take with the helper. I also like the fact that you cannot place a die on a tile that is equal or greater to a die that is already there... although you can change that with god cards.

It makes me think of Tzolkin, but without the craziness of the wheels but with an island to work with instead. :)

But it's still a point hoarding game. I would have prefered less points and more features. Like when you accomplish a goal, instead of getting points you would get a bonus somewhere, like a character appears to occupy a hut (drawn randomly or not), or collect a ressource of your choice under one of your huts again, or advance a priest on the temple track towards the left, or something.

Point hoarding is so boring.

I would never buy this game... and if I got it as a present, I would eventually modify it so that it is no longer only a point hoarding game.
