RATING: 6
This game is pretty intimidating to play for the first time (or to explain to newbies), despite the excellent iconography on the player boards.  I'm not really sure that I like the game.  There are too many ways to get points, and you can't just follow one strategy to get all your points.  The final scores are in the 130-200 range, so it's not obvious how a 6-point play now is a good idea, or will cost you a turn that you could have used to set you up better to get more points later.

There are many 6-point scoring bonuses at the end of the game, but it's not a good idea to try to pursue them all (or even several of them).  The "all huts on the board" and "all filled with men/women" do seem to work together well, but it's hard to buy jewellery every turn or completely all 9 goal tiles that you receive.  You have to hope that you'll be able to score one or two of these bonuses by the end and not worry too much if you choose early in the game to take a different path that disqualifies you from getting some of them.

One thing that is important to note is that you can only put one hut in each region of the board.  So you're only guaranteed the fish points for a particular region if you are the last player to put a hut there!  This means that it might not be a good idea to go for the big-point fishes early, or at least it would be a good idea to use a Green God card to score those big fish points when you enter those regions early.

It's also important to note that tattoos can be earned (and hence the turn order can change) based on the man and woman actions in phase B.  There are tattoo actions on some man/woman tiles, which is obvious, but more subtle is the fact that there are "build building" actions that will give you a fire bonus as a result... which can be a tattoo.

RATING: 7.5
Bora Bora sat on a wait and see list for quite a while with me, despite being a highly regarded Feld game for a couple of reasons.

First, this is really a point-salad game, with lots of choices to make all the way along in the game, quite a few possible game-end bonuses, and really only one penalty mechanism- which is really not so much a penalty mechanism as just another way of falling behind your opponents. 

This is not necessarily a turn-off or an attraction to me; I like games more integrated with theme, but a well-done palette of point-salad can still appeal. Still the mechanisms didn't create the shiny factor.

More important I disliked the artwork. It seemed unattractive to my eye, and overly bright and multi-toned. Frankly it is still too tutti-frutti for my tastes in general, but it is also a lot less offensive to the eye when playing than I expected. 

Third the tropical island theme: I like it about as well as I like Tropical Island Life Savers; not my favorite, or my first choice, but not unpleasant if you need quick candy.

So I didn't grab up a copy right away. It seems this was a mistake on my part, as it turns out I like Feld's breadfruit game a good deal.

I liked this game guardedly on a first play (with 2), but after a second play (with 4), and after a couple of minor but important rules corrected during the game, I've decided that Bora Bora will definitely rank among my top Feld games. In fact I am setting an initial score of 7.5 for the game.

This game really offers a bewildering number of choices  in general, and like Trajan does to some it can probably seem overwhelming.

The dice mechanism is subtly different; not as involving as the mancala sub-game in Trajan seems to me, but the possible play of denying others access makes die placement choices more interactive, while the God cards can be used cancel the blocking effects enough that it still has a primarily solitaire feel with two.

But in a four player game I found the struggle for dice position tighter, and more engaging than for two. The corrected scoring of fish at the end made the struggle for hut placement more critical, and in fact the game came down to a tie-break.

For four players, instead of a unfavorable first comparison with Trajan (my top-ranked Feld game), I was minded more of Macao and a similar tightness to action choices (Macao appeals to me a great deal, but it's not quite as much of a home run as Trajan).

Bora Bora deserves it's fans, because it offers a tight, worker placement, with limited actions and resources, and just enough interaction to make it interesting, but with enough options to provide alternative choices if your 1st 2nd and 3rd options aren't available for that last die. 

Like Macao, I am starting Bora Bora at a 7.5 - the game has real merit, and it's nicely thought out.  There is no one catch-up mechanism that I see, but it's still very possible to play catch-up, and even win from behind.  The interactive aspects are not a huge penalty, but they make the game interesting, and players almost never suffer for a lack of options.

Bora Bora clicks along like a Swiss watch with too many hands for me- I couldn't make heads or tails of it at first, but once I played it all began to make sense, and it moves forward at the right pace to offer a satisfying experience that lasts just the right amount of time.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
(05/17/2014) Comment based on 2 plays.

This is the wilder, crazier, younger sibling of [thing=84876][/thing], where Stefan Feld takes dice-rolling/action drafting to the next logical step of complexity and sadomasochistic pleasure.

Like CoB, dice are rolled and allocated to action spaces. However, instead of two, three dice are rolled for six turns. There are seven communal action spaces that players compete over, as in a traditional worker-placement game: 1) Use dice pips and convert into sub-actions/resources/VP; 2) Build statues for "fire" actions and compete for "god" tiles; 3) Convert land spaces with resources into villages, plus fire action; 4) Select a "man" tile; 5) Select a "woman" tile; 6) Build a hut adjacent to an existing hut via land; 7) Build a hut adjacent to an existing hut across water. What is unique about action drafting in BB is that once an action space has been occupied by a die, subsequent players can draft the action provided their pip value is less than the lowest pip value die on that space. Thus, if Player A drafts an action with a "6", Player B can draft the same action later with a "5" or less die. Player C can draft that same action with a "4", provided the "5" was the lowest die. Someone drafting with a "1" can lock out other players.

However, lower pip values generally mean less options. For example, there are six "man" and "woman" tiles available, each with a different ability. However, each tile is associated with a pip value. A "6" means a player can take any of the six available tiles, while a "1" restricts the player to only the tile associated with one pip. This makes it an interesting and delicious dilemma: either gain maximum flexibility, but allow other players access to the action OR restrict access to the action, but have less flexibility with your selected action. 

Just like the die-adjustment worker chits in CoB, BB does allow die-adjustment through "god" cards, which can be collected through Action #1, and any action that yields "fire" actions, which are generally through building statues in Action #2 and building villages in Action #3. There are two god that deal with this: 1) the "White" god, which converts any lower pip into a "6"; and 2) the "Blue" god, which allows a player to place a die equal to or greater than the lowest pip value die on a given action space. To invoke these gods, an "offering" chit must be made, which can generally be collected through Action #1, and the "fire" action.

As for scoring VP, there are generally six categories: 1) building huts on the various islands, depicted on the main board, which also yield resources; 2) building villages on the player tableaus, which also yield fire actions; 3) building and maintaining statues, whose values increase 1/2/3 per statue for the first third/second third/last third of the game; 4) number of "tattoos", which also determines the turn order for the next game; 5) individual jewelry, which can be purchased for a number of "conches" at the end of every turn; 6) satisfying personal objective tiles, which are evaluated at the end of every turn. In addition, there are extra bonus VP for having completed 9x objective tiles, collecting 6x jewelry pieces, collecting 12x resources, building 6x villages, placing 12x huts, and placing 12x "man"/"woman" tiles.

As huts are built on the island spaces on the main board, these open up spaces for "man"/"woman" tiles to occupy them, which in turn give extra actions/benefits at the end of every turn. There is a very large variety of these, which range from getting extra resources, allowing selection of additional "man"/"woman" tiles, to getting VP, and so much more. Since exactly one "man" and one "woman" tile can be activated, it is beneficial to collect both, and then have a variety to maximize flexibility. Further, if Action #1 is drafted, it is possible to "tap" these tiles on the tableau to a second position, where they give a one-time benefit, in terms of either "tattoos" for "man" tiles, or "conches" for "woman" tiles.

As can be seen from above, there are many, many complex interrelationships between all seven actions. Further, there are two spatial areas on each player's tableau, which serve as constraints for that player. Coupled with a common area where all players compete for a weak-form of area control, this is one complicated game. It took about 30-45 minutes to thoroughly go through the rules explanation, and as with any Stefan Feld game, first-plays are fraught with "What am I supposed to do?" reactions. Therefore, there is a substantial learning curve to play well, and score well in BB, as with any other Stefan Feld game. It is about finding the most lucrative combinations, given the die rolls you have made, as well as your opponents', and what actions/resources/tiles/cards they have taken before your turn. It is both exciting to see what one can do with one's die rolls, but it is equally frustrating as well, especially if the die rolls don't go favorably. That is what the god cards are for, and there are several others not discussed here, but collecting those is a task onto itself.

What is interesting here is that this does not feel like your typical Feldian "point salad", where you are trying to maximize the VP you collect in a given turn, or setting up something more lucrative several turns later. If nothing else, you can simply turn in a die and get some free VP, but there are definitively much better options available, regardless of how the others have drafted their actions. Like CoB, the VP value of completing villages goes down every third of the game, while statues go up in value. It seems there are several paths to victory, but no single path is assuredly the best, as everybody's die rolls constantly introduce chaos into the decision matrix. Further, the theme does not feel "pasted on" like earlier Feld designs; this one in BB is decidedly better integrated with the mechanisms, although there is no reasonable explanation for what the die rolls represent.

Play time is somewhat long at 2.5 to 3 hours for the first few games, but there is very little downtime, which occasional possible AP. The quality of the components is very good, typical of Alea, while the rules are somewhat verbose and dense. There is one heck of a chunky game here, and it will take more than a few plays to really get it. It is yet another Feld puzzle that begs to be explored further. It is a solid "8". Will it displace CoB or [thing=102680][/thing]? Hard to tell, as it is far less accessible than either of these two designs. Still, it seems deservedly placed in the top 100.

RATING: 7.5
I really like this game. At the time I purchased it, my collection was lacking a meatier Euro. This one appealed for three reasons: the price makes it a great bargain, the board and art are gorgeous, and I've always wanted to go to the real Bora Bora (yes, that's a silly reason to buy a game, but hey, I've had less reason for purchasing a game before!) I haven't played too many heavier Euros so I don't have a lot to compare it to at this point. The things I like are the plethora of strategy choices available (which I guess gives it that designation of "points salad") and none that, at least so far, seem overly dominant; the nice layer of player interaction from the dice placement mechanic, with the available but not spam-able options to "smooth over" dice results that don't work for you; and the gorgeous art design. The rules book takes a lot of flak, but I watched a few videos on play and didn't have any trouble grasping the game, and actually I don't think it's all that hard to grok. I'm a fan.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Fun and challenging cerebral victory points salad with pretty game board and components.  This is a tough game to teach.  The game board and each player's mat is very busy and visually overwhelming.  There are many moving parts that form a fun victory point optimization puzzle, but the different actions you can do and the feel of the game present an unfortunate unpleasant thematic dissonance.  There is no indication of why we are adding huts to the island and adding workers and collecting jewelry and adding priests to the temple track.  Look, as Euro game enthusiasts, we are often professing that the theme is of marginal importance and games should be judged on mechanics and clever game design.  I feel compelled to point Bora Bora out as an example of a game design that failed to tie in the mininimum requisite theme to inform players why we are doing the actions we choose and how they make sense as steps toward the stated goals of the game.  It's not quite enough to have a pretty board and components and clever interconnected mechanisms and resource management, area control and novel action selection.  So now that I have bitched about the ethereal theme, I still enjoy playing the game.  I love the clever interconnected mechanics.  I just wish there was a better integrated narrative to inform players why we are choosing the actions besides the obvious: to maximize score at game end.

RATING: 8
This is the first Feld game I've tried and it seems I've jumped into the deep end. Nevertheless, don't be intimidated. After about two or three rounds, the game starts to move pretty smoothly. I've taught it to several different people now and by the second half of the game they have a good handle on the rules. The biggest task is learning the iconography, which is generally well-designed and easy to understand.

Overall, this is a game about drinking from a firehose of possibilities for earning VPs. At first it feels like a motley collection of different mechanics, however the more you play, the more the subtle connections between them become clear. Thus, gameplay is all about optimizing your options. The game offers many chances to screw the other players such as blocking their action selection via placing lower-valued dice, taking a man/woman/jewelry/task tile that they need, displacing their hut (robbing them of the end-game fish bonus), and pushing their priest out of the temple (causing them to lose an end-of-round VP bonus). The god cards are a handy way to bend the rules a bit, so there may be some recourse to nasty plays. Despite all that's going on, the game is surprisingly well-balanced, with many games ending with close VP totals.

I highly recommend it, unless theme is important to you, in which case stay far away.

RATING: 8.7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.2
In Bora Bora, each player attempts to establish the best island village island by recruiting villagers, building huts, accumulating resources and fostering religion.  Each turn, players roll three dice and use the results, which can be modified by special "god cards," to take actions.  Along the way, each player is assigned various goals, the completion of which results in additional victory points.  

Bora Bora provides an enjoyable one-hour gaming experience with two players.  In particular, I like how the task goals provide direction in what could otherwise be a overwhelming choice of options.  I still prefer the mancala mechanic that Mr. Feld used in Trajan over Bora Bora's dice assignment mechanic, but really enjoy strategizing how to efficiently accumulate the most victory points with so many promising point generating actions.  At the moment, Bora Bora has a slight edge over Castles of Burgundy, but is not quite as satisfying as Trajan.  

Rating: 8.2 (Fun 8; Gameplay 9; Components 9; Theme 5)
Formula:
[Weighted rating = (((Fun*4) + (Gameplay*3) + (Components *2) + (Theme *1))/10)]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Great game, there are so many things to do and so many different ways to do them. You get pulled in a lot of different directions and it will require clever use of your actions and your bonuses to be able to do much more than half of them. You get bonuses at the end of each round depending on the people you recruit to your tribe, and you get a certain set of bonuses every time you build a part of your building and every time you make a priest. Dice are central to the game and there are better and worse rolls, but the worst rolls are either all high or all low, you want variety. Plus there are special cards you can collect and use that will even out the die rolls, among other things. These are my favorite kinds of games, the ones where you're pulled in different directions and you need to cleverly use your actions to chain together bonuses to get everything done.

This is one of the more aggressive versions of this kind of game that I've played; there are a lot of opportunities to block your opponents and really hurt their game, so that might bother some people but it really doesn't feel too bad, even as someone who generally doesn't like that too much.

RATING: 6
Bora Bora features many of Stefan Feld's typical flourishes such as interesting action selection (specifically using dice) and a point salad full of ways to score points. This is usually right up my alley, but Bora Bora falls a bit flat for me. Luck - something Feld is usually a master of keeping in check - seems to be a tad too high for my tastes between the card draws and difficulty manipulating dice (unlike Castles of Burgundy, there is something resembling an optimal dice roll). I also wasn't a fan of the objective system that forces players into certain actions and the way that players don't so much build engines but rather connect actions together (e.g. expansion allows getting a resource that can be used in the future to build and acquire another resource that can be used in the future to...). The game feels more tactical than it seems like it should be. It also doesn't scale down to lower player counts as well as his other games. The art is bright and colorful (but busy) and the game certainly provides a wealth of meaningful choices, but it lacks the satisfying feelings of finding combinations and optimizations that make his other games shine.

RATING: 8
2013-10-08

Initial Rating: 8.0 (September 2013)

What a surprise, I (generally) like Feld games, but this one exceeded my expectations by far. Perhaps it can be my favorite Feld and it is certainly the heaviest one I have played. As usual you have lots of options with very few dice to make it happen. The rules are many, but they are pretty simple, the “heaviness” comes from the choices required to play, they are quite interesting and I love them.  

The game components and art are ok, although they are better than other Alea games. I dislike the paper thin and boring theme, but the gameplay is good enough to make me like the game. 

Bora Bora is a very (typical) “Feld” game, nice implementation on using dice, it also features a track to determine turn order. There isn’t anything new going on this game however everything works perfectly together, and I had to think a lot more in this game than in the other Felds, so I really like Bora Bora. 

The game features some luck with the goals and the man/woman characters, but it doesn’t bother me much as you have plenty of opportunities to mitigate it. 

Bottom line, a must have for me, great game and I recommend it. 

Current Rating: 8.0

RATING: 5
"Boring boring" despite tight mechanics.  Pros: The graphic art and components are well done, and nice and colorful. The mechanisms in the game are well designed, and as is typical with Feld games there are a lot of choices (perhaps too many) and a lot of pathways to get points. Cons: The theme seemed pasted on, in a two player game the hut expansion mechanic does not work very well, and the game was just simply un-engaging.  We could not wait for the first game to end, it just seemed like we were performing actions for the sake of performing actions, and when we finished the game we wanted to put it away instead of having another go at it (never a good sign). Conclusion: Overall, the game is well-designed but simply just not fun.

PLUS: tons of options, strategy based on rolls, great manual
MINUS: playing time isn't small

RATING: 7
Do I need this if I have [gameid=84876]? Well, it fits the same slot in terms of length, number of players and complexity as a midweigth to heavy euro game. It also features lots of levers, systems, subsystems and a hundred ways to score victory points although usually in the hundreds and not the two-hundreds. Both games are games of maximizing efficiency. Beside that, it's a different enough game. It doesn't feel as constructive, the luck of the dice seems heavier to mitigate, it's less accessible, there's a few more possibilities to screw another player from points. In the long term, it might be the deeper game while feeling more mechanical. After one play, I prefer die Burgen von Burgund as the more cohesive game. Bora Bora - like Trajan - might be a step too far in depth by complexity although it's a good game I'm willing to play more.

RATING: 7
Már a szabálykönyv olvasgatása közben is éreztem, hogy ez nekem tetszeni fog. Pedig a körítés valójában inkább nem tetszik, de igazából nincs jelentősége, ide-oda tologatás az egész, nem sokat számít a téma.

Az akciók kockával való aktiválása zseniális szerintem, azaz minél kisebb értéked van, annál több hely közül választhatsz, viszont gyengébb az akció ereje. Ez egy nagyon konfrontatív megoldás, mert egy-egy kockával mindenki mástól elzárhatunk bizonyos lehetőségeket. Nagyon sokat számít a játékossorend emiatt.

Nem a legegyszerűbb játék, sőt, inkább hevi kategória, de emellett elfogadható a játékidő, viszont a szabálymagyarázat és setup nem kevés. Nem bármikor, de szívesen játszom még vele.

RATING: 7.3
A very typical Feld. I rate it slightly below most Felds because the main mechanism isn't anything really new or interesting - it's a not very different variation on dice placement. The actions built on the mechanism are really the usual Feld stuff, a separate track to determine turn order, a building grid that's also present in most Felds (this time it's on the personal boards) etc. etc.
Still, a seemingly well balanced, gamer-friendly optimization game; it just doesn't offer anything as novel or interesting as most previous Feld games.
Update: and yet, for some reasons, it's somehow more well-developed and interesting than most Felds.

RATING: 5
This is a Feld point salad.  There are a myriad ways to score but the main mechanic is that you roll three dice and placing them for actions.  You can get more actions by placing high value dice, but you can block a space by placing a low value.  Some actions require a minimum value to execute.  

You are collecting sets of various items that increase if you get more of those items.  

I found it about the same weight as Trajan, but lacking the excitement that Trajan brings to the game.  It is an acceptable game and I would probably play again, but I doubt I would suggest it or seek it out.  It is a generic but well designed, themeless Euro.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Another solid entry in the Alea big box series, and it's easy to see why Alea continue to keep giving the job to Stefan Feld. This is a slightly "busier" design than you would typically expect from Stefan Feld in that there's a lot going on, but it is nevertheless clearly one of his designs, with many of the trademark elements; dice with variable uses, competition for turn order, and a plethora of scoring opportunities.

I'm not totally sold on the artwork and components. Although the quality is good, the colourful artwork and small size of some components can make it difficult to take it all in. The player mats, of which nearly half is dedicated to summarising rules and scoring, is daunting at first, but thankfully the iconography is logical and consistent, so once you've heard the rules, it's pretty easy to remind yourself.

The first play through of this game was difficult, as it's very hard to gauge how everything fits together and what the good strategies are. However, subsequent plays have shown that while there's a lot going on, there is plenty of room for building good strategies. It's not the sort of game where you can do everything, so specializing is rewarded.

RATING: 6
"More" isn't always "better". Someone should say that to my friend Feld. 

This game feels like [thing=102680][/thing] (and I didn't really like that one). Lots of ways to "collect" points, but in all these options, Feld forgot to add "gaming fun". And yet I'm a fan of most of Feld's games.

Just like [thing=106217][/thing] (not a Feld game), this game tries to hide its dryness by offering you lots of components with very bright and happy colors. Theme? What? Was it supposed to have one? :-)

Off course the game mechanism is well constructed, and I'm sure there will be people that will actually like it, But I would prefer to spend the 3 hours it took (1 hour explanation + 2 hours playing time), to play some other (Feld) game.

Considering the intellectual effort you need to invest in this game, isn't it a bit stupid to let dice decide on your options? Throwing high numbers a lot, can really kill your chances.

Typically a game that, when you didn't play it for a few months, you will be enforced to reread of the rules.

I'm disappointed by this one.

Minus: 2 (too much mechanism, abstract) - 3 (high play time) - 5 - 9

RATING: 7

RATING: 3
Not my kind of game at all. Took the dude an HOUR AND TEN MINUTES to explain it enough to get us to the point of being able to play it, and even then we had no idea what was going on. Although in the game's defense he was pretty bad at explaining stuff and basically overloaded us with info. That being said, there really IS that much going on with it. There is an absurd amount of mechanics, an obnoxious reliance on obtuse iconography (looking at you, too, Race For The Galaxy), and is all limited to a total of 6 rounds, meaning that you'll never be able to utilize all of those mechanics in one game. Those are 3 huge red flags for me, personally. Others might enjoy it, for sure. But get away from me with that crap. If the idea of Terra Mystica in the Pacific Ocean sounds intriguing, this is your game. If you're like me and you can't stand Terra Mystica or other super-dense Euro games, run for the hills.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is vying for my favourite Stefan Feld game. I think it varies between this, Trajan, The Castles of Burgundy and The Speicherstadt. Probably depending which one I last played.

But boy do I hate teaching this game. For the look of the game and board/player board artwork forget point salad this game looks like Syd Barrett salmagundi salad. I saw things like this when I was experimenting in my youth! It looks like a starving perp has broken into the carribean supermarket ate as much of the fruit as possible and then done a runner from the arriving cops, vomiting this up on the way out as their overworked legs pumped their stomach into a hyperactive state.

But get past that and the game play is fantastic. Get past the fantastic game play and you realise the colourful artwork is fantastic. Get past this and you realise all those halucinations you saw on the side of your player board are actually useful summaries of what your mind is being taken through. Then you understand what is really going on in the game and you realise your mind has finally raised itself up like an overclocked deck-jacker from "Neuromancer" and achieved that unique Stefan-like state of Feldian mush and probably looks like the artwork you have just spent the last hour trying to interpret.

I digress. What I love about this game is that deep down hidden behind everything your mind is trying to compute, and make sense of, the gameplay is fairly simple. You are lead through decisions by the tasks you are given each round to complete. For new gamers this makes the game so much more a pleasant experience than say Trajan where you are often left scratching your head wondering which way to turn as you realise you are spending way to long thinking about moving small coloured pieces of wood between flat 2D cardboard bowls whilst your fellow players rampage past you like roman hordes after bread, games and wealth. Here you know in the short term what you want to do .... And yes you want to do that as soon as possible and then .... then, you want to bugger up your fellow players with cunning, devious, wily plays of your dice.

This brings us to the dice play, which is delicious. You can truly bugger up your opponent and then sit back and watch them squirm. But squirm they will and the strength of this game is there is almost always another way to get to where you want to be. Pray to whichever god takes your fancy or ask your tribesmen and women for help. It is this ability to squirm and wriggle out of tight situations and accomplish your goals which makes this game so enjoyable.

I really enjoy this game but due to the perceived heaviness of the game it doesn't come out that often.

Love it. Keeper.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My opinion of this game started pretty low, maybe near a 6, but rose steadily, and has now teetered off around an 8. What I'm really growing to love about this game is just how rewarding it is. It's really one of the hallmarks of a good Feld game: you get rewarded for doing good things. Bora Bora looks at the good things you've done in your gameplay, and says "good job, here's a small bonus for doing so well!". It's just very rewarding to play this game and always get that good feeling that you've done something right. What brings this game down slightly is the super long setup time, and how fiddly it is at times. I feel like the setup time is too long for what this game is, and there are just a few too many bits for me to like. It's not really enough to ever deter me from playing the game, just something that annoys me a little when I pull it out. I would recommend this game to anyone looking to get a deep, heavy strategy game that rewards you for planning ahead, and building an in-game engine.

RATING: 8
I enjoyed the colorful board and fun theme more than i do in most of Feld's other offerings. This game has a lot of different elements going on in it, and i found it slightly AP inducing because of the dice planning. Its core is dice placement, but you have to spend extra time planning and analyzing because in order to choose an action, the dice you place has to be a lower number than the dice that's already on that action. So there's a tight stressful element right there, choosing which order to do your actions in. Overall i felt that the thematic elements such as tattoos, conch shells, huts, and necklaces make Bora Bora a fun and enjoyable game.

RATING: 8
We played Bora Bora for the first time yesterday (16/02/13).

A first impression: Overall I think the game is very good.
It's a game where a lot is going on, multiple roads to victory and there is a lot of indirect interaction (therefore very prone to AP).
Bora Bora has a semi original usage of dice (the dice are used to activate actions, and at the same time they can limit your opponents to use these actions (can be very cutthroat) but combined with a lot of random placed tiles this makes Bora Bora a bit heavy on the luckside, especially for such a heavy game. Luckily there are cards to tweak your dice, so that's a good mechanism to counter your bad luck. 


I look forward to play this a lot the coming weeks and months

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I've refrained from buying Bora Bora for a long time, since it shares some obvious similarities with CoB and Trajan, both favourites in our collection (we also own Bruges and Luna, but rate them quite a step below the other two). I finally pulled the trigger when I got it for a good price sans shipping costs at amazon.de and have played it twice since. It's just amazing how this game clicked. We just instantly groked the rules and mechanics, who made themselved immediately recognizable as a Feld brainchild. The game was way less daunting and convoluted as I feared. I have a hard time imaging a Trajan fan who wouldn't also love this game. It has the same "minigame collection" style gameplay and agonizing decisions. Its mechanics are maybe even more interlocked as in Trajan but the weight is still comparable. It's also more colorful and less drab as Trajan and provides a nice "change of scenery". Rating this a 9 for now, a smidgen below CoB and Trajan, but that might as well just be me being careful to award it too high a score after just two games. An absolute hit for us and should be right in the ballpark of every Feld afictionado. Highly recommended!

Downgraded to 8.5 after the first shine faded a little. Still a great game and one I'd always be happy to play.

RATING: 7.5
Preliminary rating based off one solo play.  I feel like the game just overstepped on complexity to retain an enjoyable game experience.  Feld has never been known for a great theme in his games, but as games become more complicated, the theme becomes important to remember and understand how everything interacts.  I think Bora Bora could be a brilliant game for the type of gamer who can devote many plays to it in a short time.  Unfortunately, I have a lot of games with not a ton of time.  If I'm lucky, I'll get the same game to the table 3 times in a month...

Edit:
I've enjoyed this game more than I initially expected, but I'm still debating whether to keep it or not.  I simply enjoy Trajan and Macao so much more...

RATING: 5.5

"The problem is that rolling all low numbers is still far worse than rolling all high numbers, and being able to block someone out of a spot is poor compensation for an entire turn where you can basically accomplish nothing. In Bora Bora, you find yourself continually screwed, seemingly on every other turn. There are plenty of other games which have a similar limiting/frustrating feel (Agricola comes to mind), but because in Bora Bora the pips on the dice are often the cause of your screwage, it can seem arbitrary and ultimately less satisfying."

RATING: 8.7
A very typical Feld, but this time with much more interaction. This game starts with an overwhelming amount of information, but game play is actually very clean and elegant, while decisions aren't obvious - you need to look at your opponent's dice and predict what they want to do so you can place a dice of suitable value to use the actions. 

If anything, there's plenty to keep track. You need to look ahead the function of woman/man and task tile displayed each round, not to mentioned those that you have, in order to plan your move. That said, once you are familiar with the icons, you can start to formulate strategy much easier. While this game kind of awards specialization, a diversification approach is perfectly fine and scores have been close among players. 

This is one of the Feld's better game, though it may takes a play or two to truely appreciate it. The large amount of info needed also makes it slightly worse than some of his best titles that are much cleaner.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In the island of Bora, Bora there are 6 rounds. Each round you roll three dice. You place dice, one at a time on action tiles to do the action: expand by water or land, take a man tile, take a woman tile, build, put a priest on a temple or do a helper action. The higher the dice you put on the action tile, typically the higher amount of things you get to do. However, subsequent plays on that action tile can only be dice that are of lower value. 

You then decide to use one man and one woman tile (which are variants of the action tile). Finally you complete tasks, buy jewels and draw more task tiles.

Finally added to the mix are God cards which let you score fish, or modify dice, or give you more man/woman actions.  In the endgame you score points for being adjacent to fish (the last one in gets the fish points). 

There are a myriad of ways to score points, which may turn some people off. I don't mind it, but certainly this is one of Feld's more complex games. I enjoy the various challenges of trying to meet tasks, and you are jammed for actions. I'm not sure this game is that accessible to all gamers though. Still, I'd like to get in a few more plays. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Rating after one learning game and one real game. Both two player. Love it. There's an awful lot going on and loads of choices, the majority you want to do but can't! Typical Feld: dice, dozens of tiles and loads of interlinked options.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
While I don't always love a point salad, I enjoy them when they have a point.

Bora Bora utilizes the point salad method of distribution to ensure that you can win in any number of ways, and I love it!  So many games, I thought I was assured victory, but forgot all about something at the end of the game, like the fish score and someone snatches the victory.

It is a very balanced game. There are task tiles to give you some objectives, but I've won by largely ignoring these and going for other things. I just love how you can win by using any crazy combination that you can think of. Sometimes, those plans turn out to not be the best, but you never know until the end. The beauty of the game is that with no two games turning out the same, typically you have to adjust your plan based on the plans of your opponents.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5.5
A little complicated, but I liked this more than Castles of Burgundy I think. Its theme and game board felt livelier to me anyway, than Burgundy's dryness. Gameplay-wise, I thought the dice locking you out was interesting, but I thought there was a fair bit of luck in how they were rolled and what tiles came out, which strongly affects your prophesies and resources. I'm not saying this isn't a strategic game (in fact, I think you can make a fair number of strategic and tactical choices), but I do dislike how the dice can screw you a bit.

I think what I dislike about Stefan Feld's games is that it sometimes feels mechanically convoluted for the sake of gameplay. I felt like this with Terra Mystica too. I prefer simple rules with deep gameplay, but with this, there's things happening within things, affecting other things, and random things to remember. Perhaps I'm just not good at this stuff or its too complex, but I so much prefer games like Puerto Rico or Power Grid, which feel so much smoother to me. Not sure if my point gets across and would love a discussion about this, as its hard for me to pinpoint what I dislike precisely about his games and would love some talk over this. But basically, a lot of things in the game are kind of arbitrary, and there's not tie-in other than to balance the game/mechanics. I don't mind this a bit, but I think its present in a lot of his games. They also just tend to feel dry/not lively to me, compared to other euros like Agricola or Lords of Waterdeep which feels a bit more lively or engrossing.

Perhaps one way is saying that theres a lot of tiles and chits and stuff happening, so it doesnt feel that elegant when it really should be. Strategy games like Puerto Rico just feel a lot smoother and cleaner and offer a better depth to complexity/weight ratio.

Anyway, I think this game was decent if not a bit much to swallow at once. I would definitely play again if offered, though I am not craving to play it.

RATING: 7
Another Feld dice-allocation game. This one is strongly reminiscent of Castles of Burgundy, but I like it better; there is a worker-placement aspect, and more combo effects and things to do. The objectives also provide a good sense of direction.

The most interesting part is that higher/lower numbers are relevant (unlike CoB), but not necessarily better (unlike Alien Frontiers): a higher number does more, but is more easily blocked.

There is a lot to take in at first, but it hangs together nicely.

RATING: 8
Stefan Feld's heaviest (imo) game to date. So many very interconnected minigames! It's fantastic! Not Trajan fantastic (nothing is for me), but fantastic nonetheless. The dice placement aspect adds an unusually mean edge for a Feld. Stock up on god cards!

Verdict:

What I Like: Clever Feldian comboing to be had all over. The competitive dice placement action selection forces a prioritization of desired actions. Therefore competitors' possible choices place constraints (imagined or actual) on each other, while the game itself is fairly generously open handed. All parts feel different yet intergrate smoothly into a cohesive whole. Beautiful, if shallow, theme.

What I Don't Like: This game is on the fiddly end chits-wise, even for a Feld design.

RATING: 8.5
心得:
規則細項繁多
只開過兩人場，人多應該會玩得更擠
每一次的任務基本上就限制了走的方向
也導致每場策略應該都會不一樣
這應該是遊戲的階段性目的強烈 
讓雖然選項繁多
但是玩家可以快速作出選擇
規則:
2p:5塊 ⇨ 幫手 寺廟 建築 男人/女人 路/水
3p:6塊 ⇨ 幫手 寺廟 建築 男人 女人 路/水
4p:7塊 ⇨ 幫手 寺廟 建築 男人 女人 路 水
* 隨機放置魚版塊，男人女人版塊，珠寶版塊，起始每個人一個淺綠備任務跟兩個深綠背任務，剩下的深綠任務洗勻，抽人數+2放在版圖上，神明版塊放六塊，卡片開五張在旁邊，房子有兩間在右下角
*每人起始 一個神明版塊， 兩張抽的神卡(隱藏)，兩供品跟一個順位版塊(隨機抽)，照順位疊在刺青軌，反順位放一個小屋在魚版塊是1的位置
A 全部骰子一起擲，照順位輪流放一顆骰到行動版塊動作，骰子要比行動版塊上的都還小
*擴展行動 通過路或水蓋小屋到相鄰的位置，把該區的小屋推開(跟最後記分有關)，可用神卡記魚的分，骰點要大於路徑顯示
*男人女人行動 拿男人女人放在自己版塊上，要有空位且還不用下移
*幫手 根據圖版顯示可使用點數換取不同東西
*寺廟 放人去骰子對應格子 並拿火焰獎勵，有人往後推
*建築 建設少於骰點的建築版塊到有兩個相鄰資源的神壇 記分跟拿火焰獎勵
*魚行動 可隨意放骰一顆骰兩分
B 使用一個男人跟一個女人行動 (能力相同會放大)
C 刺青軌記分 重新分配順位> 寺廟記分 神明版塊給僧侶最多的，平手最左邊的拿>每個人買一個當輪可買的珠寶> 完成一個任務(沒辦法完成得零分棄掉一個任務，完成任務不用支付，只要擁有)選一個新任務(第六輪結束也要)
* 遊戲結束時每個人可以把最後三塊任務完成
* 使用神明卡時要付一個供品，可用兩張一樣的當作另外一種神明(神明版塊不用)，綠色神民可把男人女人行動總合後*2或是多啟動一個，每張神明卡一輪只能用一張，除了最後三個任務可用三張以外
最後記分
1.神明版塊一個兩分
2.魚版塊記分(只有最後建的人有)
3.珠寶分數
4.額外任務每個六分

RATING: 9

RATING: 8
It's another points salad game, but it's got some very likeable bits, including how each turn you have three mini-quests to work towards, giving you focus and reason to drive down this VP path or that one for a bit, and then delve down another. Which I much prefer over others in the genre where you just move to a strategy that others aren't doing and hope to get lucky with your efficiency. There's luck to manage in what action spaces you'll get to action, limited by dice rolls and what others take ahead of you, but there's so many ways to progress, there's rarely a feel of getting hosed. Some of the end-game points are at-the-limit, where missing out on something one turn can lose you points, but everyone faces the same issue and insurance options abound. Lots and lots of moving parts, which means lots of rules and a time investment accordingly, but it also means there's lots of exploration available to those who love point salad games.

RATING: 7
I can't understand all the buzz around this game. This is a solid Eurogame but nothing more.

- many paths to victory
- many ressources to juggle
- nailbiting decisions
- zilions of different tokens that might or might not come into play and therefore offer always a different way to play the game

RATING: 7
Based on one play, so take with a heaping spoonful of salt:

The use of dice to determine the availability and strength of actions is brilliant.  I enjoyed the tense decisions of how to use the dice each round.  I do think there are perhaps at least one too many sub games going on.  Some of the scoring elements seem to be a tad silly (my hut is in this box at the end of the game so I get points for that fish), but all the elements with a bit of imagination can be reasonably tied together.  The game is certainly not elegant as there are tons of bits that must be set up and and reset every round.  It could easily be argued the game is bloated.  Regardless, my play of it was quite fun and I would gladly play again, but I think I will remain content to play someone else's copy.

UPDATE:  On a second playing (still someone else's copy) it seemed a little less bloated.  I enjoyed my second play even more than my first.  Bumping it up one notch.

RATING: 7
I've heard this game referred to as a "point salad"--like Castles of Burgundy, there are a ton of different, but somewhat interconnected, ways to score points.  However, I felt that this game suffered from being less well-integrated than Burgundy.  You draft various goal tiles that you simply have to complete in a timely fashion if you want to be competitive, which drives your strategy to a large degree.  However, the different paths you can pursue in the game is so multitudinous that it's difficult to tie everything together into a master plan.  I liked the clever dice mechanism, but it was just one tactical aspect of a much larger game.  While this is certainly a good game that I'd like to play more, I think it suffers from a lack of streamlining in the same way that a well-known author can publish a monster book and largely bypass the editorial process.

(2 plays)

RATING: 8
1) Ik like games of Stefan Feld really a lot
2) I sold 6 copies of this game on 1 day, I've never done this in the past 4 years!
3) The average rating is > 8.5 at this moment (17/02/2013)

All this made I played this game with very high expectations. And I must admit that I was somewhat disappointed after my first play. What I missed, and for me is typical for Feld, is that 1 original gameplay-element that makes the game really others then others. For me Bora Bora is a sort of Trajan (for example Status track  Senate; hyperfocus on only a few of different possilities to score points) with a flavor of The Casltles of Burgundy (dice to activate actions; the earlier you built your buildings, the more points you get). Off course Trajan and Castles of Burgundy are some (very) good games, so there is nothing wrong with recylcing some of those principles. But it somewhat bothers me that an author recycles (so soon) his game principles, it reminds me of what Reiner Knizia is doing lately and I think we can all agree that it went downhill very fast with the quality of the games he releases lately by doing that.

Maybe just my expectations were too high. My guess is that my rating can still go up, but certainly won't decrease :D

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Feld using dice in another interesting way. Basically worker (dice) placement where the rolled value limits the dice that can be placed on the same action space by other players. Creates nice tension in the placement phase. 

Additional actions can be taken each turn based upon collected tiles (man and woman tiles). 

Many different interconnected actions that lead down different paths to VP accumulation. Pleasantly thinky puzzle aspect to each game. Not quite a brain-burner but definitely a brain warmer.

When playing with less than 4, not all  the tiles are revealed and so you can't bank on a particular strategy with the tiles.

Bad rolls can be somewhat mitigated but multiple bad rolls per game can destroy your chances of winning. Not sure that there isn't an optimal strategy here. Build buildings as soon as possible, get a man and woman that helps with this, and complete huts in the latter game. Exploit priest rack whenever possible. Of course, everyone else will be trying to do the same and therein lies the beautiful struggle.

If you like Feld, you should like this; although, it's a bit more rules intensive than most of his games.

Enjoyable!

RATING: 8
red 60

RATING: 7
This game has the action variation of Trajan, but the theme works together, and different actions influence each other from time to time. It has the dice determining your actions mechanic of Burgundy, but it's player driven rather than game mechanics driven, where in Bora, you get to take an action so long as no player as assigned a lower die value to that action yet (and the higher the die value, the more options you have for resolving that action). I think hands down this is the best Feld game I've played yet, and I'm very pleased he seems to be building and polishing his good ideas from previous works.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:nostar: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:nostar: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::star::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: N/A
one of feld's best designs, but also maybe his meanest
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Collect victory points by going after them in many different ways. Each of the ways allow for bonuses
Expansion in territories with a fish bonus, and clearing areas to recruit villagers. Villagers who produce various resources, money or points to modify the turn order. building which give god and money bonus, priest track giving god bonuses, buying jewelry, completing collection tasks.

If you can specialize and accomplish a specific victory task every turn you get a bunch of bonus victory points at the end of the game, miss the target one turn and it is out.

The most interesting part of the game was the use of the dice as the action selection mechanism. Lower numbers are less flexible, but lock out opponents. Higher numbers give you more results but allow for easier opponent play. 

It's a very busy game with lots of moving parts, and is hard to explain to new players. 
Ultimately the game too much going on to enjoy it. It wants you to be strategic, but the turn mechanism is very tactical. 
Trajan and Castles of Burgundy are much better examples of this type of game 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Pros:
1. Holy Option Batman! There is no shortage of options in this game.
2. Dice Placement. I love dice placement. What this game does well is it make low value and high value rolls worth something. 
3. No Focus. This is a game where focusing on only one piece of the puzzle will likely lead to a loss. You need to at least dabble in every part of the board to have a chance at winning.

Cons:
1. Long Teach. There's a ridiculous amount of options and subtleties to each one. New players will need at least one play to get a handle on this game.
2. Long Play. This game take a while to play. With so many options, you're bound to run into a few points in the game where a player hits an AP wall. They just stare at the board trying to maximize. This causes the game to grind to a hault.
3. Long Setup. There's a lot of stuff in this game. It'll take at least 10 minutes to set up. Maybe less if you get a lot of people in on it.
4. Theme. There is a theme, but it's just layered on. Like most Feld games, this game uses theme more as a way to make referencing portions of the game easier, not as a way to drive any sort of story.

Summary: This is a very typical Feld game. It's good and I'd be willing to play it again. However, there are better Euros (even better Feld games) that achieve the same level of thought and strategy that do not require the same amount of time investment as Bora Bora.

RATING: 9
An amazing, yet sometimes frustrating game (but still in a good way). One move can often ruin your clever plans, so in essence this is a tactical game that forces you to be very adaptable and keeps you on your toes since the beginning till the very end.

Once again, Mr. Feld proves he is the king of dice - both high & low rolls have multiple important uses. All the mechanics are tightly interlinked and often you have to take several steps to achieve the desired outcome (which is, I think, one of the biggest differences between [i]Bora Bora[/i] and [i]Castles of Burgundy[/i], where getting the points is rather straightforward). Sometimes this can result in analysis paralysis, since no action is guaranteed - it's really easy to loose an opportunity to do certain things if your opponents block you, by chance or by malice. 

After my first games I was really on the fence about this one, but with each play it felt better and better, and now I can say this is one of the best Felds I have ever played. Tight, taxing, absorbing and really satisfying in the end.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
As a big Feld fan, I was really looking forward to this. It's certainly a very good and polished game - an 8 is a pretty good rating for me - yet I haven't found myself wowed by it on any of my 3 plays to date. It'll probably see little play in our regular group, as one of them (generally an Ameritrasher, although he plays plenty of Euros with us and even seems to enjoy some of them!), absolutely hated it after the first 2 plays, and one other also disliked it. Indeed, it was another 2 years before my wife persuaded them to play it for the third time (in June 2015) - one of them even won that game, but once again neither was enthusiastic.

The game has interesting new mechanics (what else would you expect of Feld), a seemingly wide space of possible strategies to follow, and many "small" but crucial tactical decisions. And the components are great quality, far better that all other Alea games I've played. Based on my plays so far, I don't see it displacing my favourite Felds (most notably Trajan, and actually I was and remain more impressed with Amerigo of his 2013 releases), but it's a game that I would still be keen to get some more plays of.

RATING: 5.9
(+) Plays quickly and smoothly
(+) Clear and structured gaming rules. All information printed on player boards
(+) Game has the potential to play out differently every time. Depending on how the game develops in the first two rounds, different strategies may become appealing.

(-) Awfully boring (or rather non-existing) theme
(-) Game might be a little too luck-dependant. Some mechanics (like trying to win all assignments) can end up screwing you for reasons you can not fully control. Not gaining those 6 bonus points should nog cause you to lose the game, but it feels rather disappointing.
(-) This game is too much of a point salad. Everything scores you points. Chosing the safe path and scoring them immediately usually scores you a little less compared to when you risk postponing your investment in order to try to score a little more points later on (however, due to some luck-dependance you often have no real control over your possibility to score later on). For starting players it is impossible to make out which type of goods or actions will score you maximum points. Some good strategies can turn out to be impossible to execute to the max due to the (sometimes totally random) development later on in the game

RATING: 8
A clever use of dice, with the whole dishes and sink thrown in on making VPs along the way and at the end. Typical Feldian style. I usually like the main mechanism, but the little things that get you vps, bits turn me away. I still like the mancala in Trajan best for the main driving mechanism for his games; maybe because for once he didn't use dice on that one. Love the look of it.

edit:I'm not a fanboy of Feld's designs, but I played Trajan and loved the depth of things and connections, but it felt a little disconnected. Bora Bora has this same depth, but the disconnect is not there. It is a good amount of choices in just the right amount of time. Plays 2 players well enough, but shines with more if you can find a couple more. 

There are a bunch of ways to gain victory points as in true Feld style, but there aren't too many that I felt everything I did would net me vps. It appears each way to get vps that are significant, requires 2 steps--not just one.

I didn't think I would like Bora Bora, but it actually was fun trying to puzzle out each round how to get victory points.

RATING: 5
Add one to the rating if everyone already knows how to play and we can skip the 30+ minutes it takes to teach this game well.

I think Trajan/Bora Bora marked an inflection point in the hobby, where complexity and fiddliness became, for some, a mark of distinction. Not a trend I approve of, of course. I think Feld is a genius, but I don't really care for games of this kind. I do think Bora Bora represents the best of a bad breed. I also think the legion of much-less-talented imitators that Feld has spawned has the potential to be harmful to the hobby on the whole. Not that this is All That Serious. First-world problems, and all that :laugh:

RATING: 7
Another Feld classic! This is a board game that at first sight looks more complex than it really is. Core game play does rely on dice rolls and I have played at least once where a continuous streak of extreme low rolls ruined my chances to be competitive (in this game it seems that a good spread of dice numbers are preferable to all low or all high rolls). I have a dislike for games where the capriciousness of the dice gods play an overly important role. However, in this game there are more than enough other redeeming factors to make it a title that I will happily play whenever the opportunity presents itself.

But I think as I understand the game more, the differences will be subtle, and finding ways to inch ahead will be interesting to discover.

RATING: 8

That said, a bad roll at the wrong time can certainly result in a loss of 10 to 15 points which can easily be the difference between a winning/losing score.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Played once with 2P, thrice with 3P and once with 4P. Bora Bora features a rather clever dice placement system, in which dice are used to claim actions. As expected of any serious dice-driven Euro, dice have their uses regardless of the value rolled – high values yield more choices, but low values block higher values from being placed. However, the mechanism isn't entirely robust, because poor rolls may force a player into sub-optimal moves through no fault of his own.

The rest of the game is a web of interlocking mechanisms. The task tiles are quite fascinating, because they evoke the puzzle optimisation from [thing=31594][/thing], but everything else is not individually notable. As with Feld's other heavy designs, players are expected to orchestrate a sequence of actions to crank out a few VP. The difference though, is that the relatively high degree of player interaction and overlap of mechanisms in Bora Bora makes this feat fairly frustrating to pull off. (Interaction here comes in the form of displacing opponents' huts and priests.)

In the end, I feel that the elements which are remotely interesting end up being lost amidst a sprawling network of tattooing and shell collecting. Bora Bora is still passable for a complex Euro, but it otherwise falls into the bottom tier of Feld's output for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Feld is a popular designer for good reason and Bora Bora is yet another solid entry to his portfolio.  This game blends great art/components with a simple dice placement mechanic and the result is a surprisingly deep strategy game. The setup can be a pain, however, as there are many many tiles to lay out.

RATING: 7.5
2-4, 90m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Another great Feld game, if a little similar to many of his games (most of which I like which is probably why I'm all for this one as well).

In true Feld fashion this game has a unique dice gimmick that makes up most of the game play.  At the beginning of a round each player will roll 3 dice and in turn order place these dice on action spaces.  Where the gimmick comes in is higher pip values give you more options or outright better effects from these action spaces but all future dice placed there have to be lower then the lowest one placed.  So, for example, placing a 1 on a space early wouldn't give you a very powerful effect but would also prevent anyone else from placing there.  I love rolling dice and I especially love unique, fun dice mechanics.

The actions are, also in true Feld fashion, just different ways to score victory points on this themed island paradise of the titular Bora Bora.  This I love as well because it presents varied paths to victory and all of them appear viable.

The boards in this game are a bit smaller than I would like and some of them are cluttered to hell with symbology to remind you about conversion rates and special rules.  Other than that though the game looks pretty good.

While it may feel kind of samey if you've played other Feld games I think the unique dice mechanic and new victory point actions give it enough personality and charm to be another great game from this talented designer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Well I am not a huge Feld fan but i always enjoy trying out his new games. Lets see if this is a keeper.

Rules read though this looks 'Feldian' - balancing but specialising in lots of point scorers, own player boards, little incremental bonus actions which are key in Man/Woman section and Fire Bonus. Interaction looks minimal but interesting map and Roma like dice placement, and certainly more interesting than CoB or Trajan

Production values are high - quite colourful design. 

Theme - as ever a total paste on. Could be anything but I guess that does not matter in a Feld game

I am looking forward to trying but suspect that the high scores and many ways of scoring will make close end results as lacking in meaning as Castles of Burgundy.

Anyway game play is the thing so lets see.

---------------

First impressions were not horrible. The action selection mechanic forces interaction, however the gods let you get round it. A little too easily I felt. There is incredible pressure from the beginning to both score and build an engine - or at least find a specialisation. 

Like other Feld games the number of mechanical devices are frustrating witho ut having a thematic hook - for example why does building give you a fire bonus and not expanding?
Alea have really upped their game on the production and the extra cost compared to say Die Burgen is well worth it for a design that has o much in it. My rating is not an evaluation of how good the game is.

--------------------

2nd play and having now explained the rules to 5 different people I realise this is one of Feld's more complex games. The first game is really just a learning exercise as its impossible to see all the possibilities yet alone the things you score for. In my second play I kept wishing for a god that lets you play a dice and then turn it to a '1' - the action selection is just not quite as interactive enough ('Vanuatu' style) as I'd like as the gods dig you out of any hole. The game is very Feldian - you need to plan a series of linked steps from beginning to end. Planning partly will be driven by the opening tasks and the men/women tiles open to you. The end game bonus for completed sets means you can't change course half way through the game. This means players only brush up against each other if they share the same goals- maybe competition for the islands or jewellery. However even if these minor interactions. Don't go your way there is always a god to help you out. The game consumes a lot of brain power to go through a lot of ways of achieving the same thing - because so many of the games actions can get the same result - want a resource? Explore, helper or man or women will get you there, more subtle is the most efficient use of how you achieved this - I'm not sure if this is a hallmark of a good design or a hodgepodge.

------------------------------------------

why when you create an interesting core mechanic (dice must be lower to take an action) do you then make so many ways of circumventing this?

RATING: 3.5
Convulted, dry and boring would be how I describe Bora Bora.

Lots of mechanics and mini-games (à la Trajan) with nothing to tie them together. Surprising amount of randomness considering the heaviness of the game.  You can see that some design choice; manly gods card that let you change actions and the helper action tile that let you trade any(!) ressources following a ratio; comes from the unforgiveness of the draw.

Even considering this, everything in the game makes points and there is no bad action.  You probably won't be able to follow your plan (which you have to set at the beginning of the game to have the final bonus), but you aren't really set back; you'll still move forward.

The game is not broken or badly developed, it will appeal to a certain type of gamers (it clearly does considering its ranking), but I would surely like to never have to play this again (hence to low rating).

RATING: 7.6
1500，附收納盒。
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Perhaps the zenith of Feld's "point-salad" approach, Bora Bora is nothing if not overwrought. The core mechanism -- players roll three dice and action spaces are taken by placing one, lower than any die already there; high numbers are more powerful but harder to place -- is interesting enough, though to me it feels as though the core point of interest, the blocking, is a watered-down imitation of the action-selection from Vanuatu. And of course, players can place higher dice anyway by playing a "blue god" and an offering, or a wild god tile; it's more about risk and efficiency than genuine screwage.

As in his other games, efficiencies are layered on efficiencies. The most egregious addition in this one is, I think, the "building" minigame: resources are placed on squares on a 4x6 grid and buildings must replace two adjacent resources. This adjacency rule exists for the sole purpose of adding additional considerations to the "expansion" minigame, one already overloaded -- you don't just have to worry about whether you can pay the dice cost on the correct action, the in-game points, the end-game points and the paths opened up from the new space, but also whether its resource will be useful for building. It's just unnecessary: complexity for its own sake.

Perhaps my biggest issue with the game is that, moreso than any of his others, the system feels very driven by short-term goals. The (often competing) incentives to complete task tiles add another wrinkle to any calculation of an action's expected value, but the bonuses are significant enough that players will try to complete all nine; likewise, the specialization bonuses (6 jewelry tiles etc.) are significant enough that choosing any given strategy early may begin to feel like a straightjacket.

Sure, the key here is the much-loved "want to do more than you have actions available", but right now it's just a little too much. My hope is that, like Trajan, where the mancala went from impossibly brain-burning to relatively simple in the space of a few short games, eventually the mechanisms will fade into the background and bring the player interaction, unusually strong for Feld, to the fore.

RATING: 5
My least favorite Feld. I thought my brain circuits were going to overload at the 20-minute rules explanation, and then the game itself was overwhelming and I forgot several details, including the most important one which was to get your priests on the temple track. At times the game encourages bad AP because there are so many (too many) ways to score. This is all after one play, so I'm sure subsequent games will be less aggravating since I won't be completely floundering. The theme did nothing for me, and board was visually overloaded.

Also, the concept that getting more tattoos makes you the leader is just plain wrong-headed and culturally insensitive on two levels. First, status in Bora Bora may be indicated by the type of tattoos, not the sheer number of them. More importantly, tattoos *express* (among many other things) status, they do not confer it.

RATING: 8
While I don't always love a point salad, I enjoy them when they have a point.

Bora Bora utilizes the point salad method of distribution to ensure that you can win in any number of ways, and I love it! So many games, I thought I was assured victory, but forgot all about something at the end of the game, like the fish score and someone snatches the victory.

It is a very balanced game. There are task tiles to give you some objectives, but I've won by largely ignoring these and going for other things. I just love how you can win by using any crazy combination that you can think of. Sometimes, those plans turn out to not be the best, but you never know until the end. The beauty of the game is that with no two games turning out the same, typically you have to adjust your plan based on the plans of your opponents.

RATING: 7.5
I'm holding my rating for more plays.

So far, I like this game.  It has the multitude of choices I enjoy in Feld games.  It flows nice after a few games and you begin to see the bits and pieces relate to each other.  A few things in the game get forgotten by us (god tiles, fishing, red god).  Mainly the little add on points.  They help a lot!

I need more plays to see if I can counter this.

Fun to play but still investigating.

update:  More plays revealed ways around this.  Unfortunately my wife does not enjoy the mental labor needed to plan in this game.  It's definitely interesting but a little to chunky and procedural.  I think more players might make it a touch better and with gamers who want to get into the process.

RATING: 10
Bora Bora eats Caylus for breakfast and defecates Power Grid while Le Havre is trembling in the corner. Seriously speaking this is a great gamer's game. I'm not the one to love dice, but in this game they work. Interesting dice placement instead of worker placement. If you roll low numbers, you are more likely to get to do anything you want. If you roll high numbers you get to do powerful actions. So everything is good in a way and luck doesn't play the biggest role. You can also influence your die rolls with cards. Dice force you to think of optimal tactical decisions while executing your strategy. 

Lots going on in this game. I love how it looks! I tend to like Feld's designs and it's nice to finally have one that is visually pleasing as well. 

Get bags to store the tiles in. Shortens setup time significantly.

Update: Yep, it's a 10.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
JAF, JAE - Just another Feld, just another Euro. 

At first glance, it looks complex and like you'll have to remember a million things, but the game itself is pretty straightforward. There are lots of ways to score points, but the dice can truly hurt you. If you have played his other games, you'll find a few similar feeling mechanics re-used, but the game itself is new. And yeah, the theme could have been anything. In fact, a space/robot theme might have been much more awesome.

RATING: 4
This seems like a very typical Feld game: intricate and disparate methods of scoring very small amounts of points over the course of a set number of game rounds. Your goal is to optimize your point-scoring potential each turn. Fans of so-called "multiple paths to victory" type games will be pleased. I will not.

As Feld games goes, there does seem to be a greater amount of overlap amongst the many different "mini-games," but this wasn't enough to save Bora Bora for me. It felt very much like other Feld games. I enjoyed Trajan more because it at least had an interesting method of action selection (the Mancala track). Bora Bora is a more typical action selection mechanic where you place one of your "workers" on an action card and then take that action. Yawn. The substitution of dice for workers is not that interesting, and can often be frustrating because the luck of your dice roll can have a detrimental effect on your strategy.

Overall, the race to optimize and score struck me as very, very typical. It's not a broken game; it just didn't have anything to make it stand out or excite me. I have no desire to play this again.

RATING: 6
When it comes to Stefan Feld games, this is not one of his best in my opinion. I prefer Macao, Trajan and Castles of Burgundy. Apart from Trajan,I'd say that his games rather tend to focus on a tactical scheme,which means quite a bit of randomness. And I think that Bora Bora excels them all in this category. Random placement of men, random placement of women, random placement of tasks, random placement of fishing grounds in the beginning, random placement of treasures,random drawing of god cards. Long-term planning just isn't possible, especially when never knowing where your coplayers will place their dice (and block) which options. You have to adapt, adapt and once again adapt to the new situation - so the game can be prone to AP. The game does not have a problem with low rolls of the dice but I'd say it has the opposite. If your dice show high results several turns in a row you are reliant on specific god cards in combination for them to be effective, you don't have that problem with low rolls. But that alone wouldn't make me rate the game "low". And it's not the typical themelessness either. What I'm missing here is the innovation. The "dice worker placement" is nothing but an inverted version of Alien Frontiers "dice worker placement system" on some of the locations. That said you can grasp with every decision you make how much thought has been put into this game. Still, not convinced by this one!

RATING: 9
