=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

Bora Bora is definitely a Feld game. It has similarities to Trajan and Castles of Burgundy, but yet is a distinct game in its own right. Like Trajan it has a number of ways to get points. However, unlike Trajan, where you are free to choose whichever path you want to pursue points, Bora Bora gives you some goals and end game bonuses that will direct your play. Additionally the various areas of the game tie together much better than Trajan and overall the game feels more cohesive. The similarity to CoB lies in the dice which determine what you do. It's significantly different in that CoB forces you to use dice for specific spots (taking or placing), whereas dice are used for actions in Bora Bora and theoretically any die in can be used for any action. The constraint in this game is that you must place a lower number than other's dice to take an action, but lower numbers are less effective. Between the various actions you can choose and the ability to divinely circumvent a bad roll of the dice, I feel like it's less dependent on dice luck than CoB. So while it pulls some elements from other Feld games, the dice action mechanic is new and interesting and it all falls together fairly well to create something unique and fun. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
[thing=127060][/thing] is a game in the same feel of the one found in [thing=84876][/thing] - almost everything you do will earn you points, the thing is to discover the best way to gain points. There are lots of things to do: build, acquire workers (men and women), put priests in the temple, gather resources, move, buy jewelry, activate workers, use cards (paying offerings) or god tiles to bend the usual rules, complete tasks, earn shells (money), advance on the turn order track (for position and, of course, points) and a few other possibilities.

It is a game with lots of actions to do and can be hard to look beyond the matrix of points in order to see the coding that will allow you to do better than others, being one with the points and controlling them to do your bidding.

[thing=127060][/thing] requires proper thinking and a good deal of timming: it happened often that one or two players would sigh and complain that with "just one more action" everything would fall in place. This could lead to some major AP, as players try to look into the void in the hopes of understanding what to do, in which order and to which effect, but the dice help to act against, as they are hateful limitations to what one can do.

The dice placement in [thing=127060][/thing] is clever: both high and low numbers can be equally useful, so whatever one rolls, there are ways to use the numbers to a good effect: high number allow to do better actions, getting the most of them - more options, better position, greater possibilities; but bigger is the room for other players to use the same place to an effect close to what you did. The lower numbers made for, usually, worst actions, but can prevent others from using the same spot. This makes [thing=127060][/thing] more interactive than [thing=84876][/thing], as there is not only the denial of items (which is the apex of interaction in CoB - taking a tile someone else might want), there is also the denial of actions - it would be as if using a low die in order to block a whole section of tiles in CoB. Since this translates to a more mean game, feelings can be hurt more (I did some nasty blockings when I could and there was wailing, tears and much sorrow in the land), so [thing=84876][/thing] might not be a 2 players game as good as CoB.

In the end of the game - after six rounds - players will score some more points if they accomplish some feats: fulfilling all the tasks, have 6 jewels, put all the huts in the board, and so on. The player with the most points will win.

[floatright][ImageID=1564158][/floatright]I must say I didn't want to play [thing=127060][/thing]. I was scared. The player aid looked to me as a work of a mad man, with so much going on that a simple glimpse of it could make you crazy. But I played Gurps Supers, so I knew I was able to deal with lots of fiddly and options. I manage to withstand the rules explanation, saw the inner workings of the construct and the components of the salad. It doesn't seemed all that bad, even if I prefer thematic meat with gravy events. Everything looked to make some sense, and the player aid was then less Hieronymus and more Bosch (tools).

The game flow is OK, with everyone using a single die in their turn, basically the same of what happens in [thing=171623][/thing], but even simpler; and definitely faster than the turns in CoB, as in this, even when using a single die, there can be several effects activating, like putting a Castle, then using a brown tile, which then activates something else, and so on. In [thing=127060][/thing] I didn't see combos of this sort - there is a progression in the in the game, but a soft one, and the things you are doing in the earlier rounds will be very close to the ones you will be doing in the last round, as they doesn't really became stronger or chainable (I guess this is a word). On the contrary - you will have less and less options as turns go by, since your huts will already be in places, your spots for workers could be filled, your workers already activated, you might not have more room for resources, etc. Though this also happens in [thing=171623][/thing] and [thing=84876][/thing], but with yellow tiles in the latter and with the incoming of houses in smaller cities in the former, things can go in a crescendo, even if the options get more restrict.

In the end the play doesn't finish with a bang - just a tally.

[thing=127060][/thing] is a smart game, heavier than both [thing=171623][/thing] and [thing=84876][/thing], but is less scary to learn and play than I thought. Everything works well. However, my tastes don't fit very much to this type of game anymore - I once liked the sort more, but my experiences push me towards other types of games, usually not those involving optimization in getting points. Still, is a game I would be willing to play every now and then - but I prefer [thing=171623][/thing] and [thing=84876][/thing] over it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Trying this because I can't shake the suspicion that it might be fun, despite serious potential downsides: too heavy, not elegant, somewhat abstract, not focused. I have a nagging Feld itch that's hard to scratch.

...Ok, this was more fun than expected. It's mostly due to only getting three measly actions between check-ins (i.e. where you need to have satisfied one of your tasks). This makes the game feel tense and also simple and smooth, even though there are a number of moving pieces. The three tasks you have at any moment also help make the game feel far more focused than it would otherwise (similar to the disasters in ITYOTD, which also lets you do extremely little on a given turn).

The feeling of interactivity is not all that strong, also similar to ITYOTD: You draft actions WP style and can block others, but mostly you're just trying to avoid screwing yourself over.

The conceit is that lower die values have better priority (and thus blocking ability) while high values get better results. However, in almost all situations a high value just gets you a greater selection from a random selection of things, with no guarantee that there's anything "better" available for a high-valued die.

Bora Bora is also a bit of a point salad, making ideal strategies less obvious. Some tension is lost when you realize that the game's options don't all interlock like an economic engine, and that ignoring one element of the game may not kill you, or even be much different from ignoring a different one.

The game feels noticeably random, like most of the other Felds I've played. I appreciate them for the experience and don't feel compelled to study the strategy.

The two spatial elements of the game are rather minor. The ceremonial spaces on the individual player boards could easily be implemented in a different way with a similar outcome. The map that players expand over has no blocking of other players, and the curious property that you're rewarded for being the [i]last[/i] person to reach a region, so that there is no feel of a race.

...I am disturbed by the possibility that the short-term goals (which I'm inclined to appreciate as adding direction to the game) actually drive the game and play the players. While you do draft most of the goals you'll have, you usually just pick whatever looks the most feasible.

...Main sentiment in hindsight is dislike of the weak interconnection of the Feldian minigames. This has almost cured me of my desire to try Burgundy and Trajan.

RATING: 8
This is a phenomenal game. The game that Castles of Burgundy wanted to be. While Castles of Burgundy suffers from 'point salad' syndrome in that almost everything will get you points, Bora Bora is a much deeper game, as players have to work toward specific strategies in order to win instead of simply taking tiles from the board every turn, then scoring points just for playing tiles on the board. The game is designed so that there is almost always something you can do with your roll, and with clever planning, you will be able to use almost any roll. One of the best heavy Euros I've ever played, and easily the best game of 2013.

RATING: 10
My favorite Stefan Feld game.  Competitive without being too in-your-face.  Lots to manage, but the tasks narrow your focus.  I love the way both high and low dice can be useful.  It also scales well at all player counts.  And while it's still a "points salad" Euro, I personally feel like the theme is better incorporated into the mechanics than Burgundy or Trajan.  My only (minor) complaint is that the rules do a poor job of explaining how the game is supposed to play (especially the very important god cards)... highly recommend a gameplay video when learning this one.  Overall, I LOVE this game!

RATING: 7
In a lot of ways, Bora Bora is a bunch of nonsense. The player boards are loud and busy, while there is so much information displayed that the game seems nigh impossible to learn.

Once you stumble through a round or two, you realize that the core of it is a very simple game and then the player boards will make a lot more sense after your third turn or so. Still, the puzzle the game throws at you is very tense. You have a lot of goals to achieve, and not much time to do it.

Overall, I think this is a solid game. Easier than it looks, yet still hard to score well.

RATING: 8

ESSEN 2013
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
3
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.3
Generally we love games that are designed by Stefan Feld. Usually if a game is designed by this man, there are a lot of small rules, which give a player lot of possible choices to score points. We call them gather-games, and we love it, as most of the time it’s unknown until the end of the game who will win and there are a lot of different ways to win. We were looking forward to this game, and were very disappointed when it wasn’t released at last year’s Spiel. We saw a prototype of it (which looked very much like the final version of the game), and the look of that, and the, positive, responds of the few people who were able to play it at the fair, resulted in the fact that we even became more interested in this game. Days were counting towards the end of February, when it would be released. We decided to pick this up mid April, but by the end of March, we noticed that almost everywhere the game was sold out. When we saw 1 copy of the game on April 1st at a fair, we immediately bought it, to save it until mid April as a present for my birthday. Within 5 minutes the only table which Bora Bora was available for playing, was free. We sat down and we got a fantastic explanation of this game.

The first look onto the game-board, wasn’t to bad. A much of the graphics become clear, just by looking at it. Then we looking to the small player-boards, and we were overwhelmed by the huge amount of information that is placed on it. After everything was explained, it became clear that the left-most quarter of the player board is just an overview of all the possibilities of the game. A game takes 6 rounds to play, and each round consist of 3 phases.

Phase 1: Placing the action dice

All players roll their three action-dice simultaneously. Then the first player, places the first die and accomplishes the corresponding action. There are a couple of small rules for placing a die. A die can be placed on every action-tile, but the value of it, has to be lower then the value of any die on it. Also the value of the placed die, determines how big the effect of the action is. In general, the lower the value of the die, the worse the effect or the options it provides will be. (but also, the lower the number of the placed die is, the harder it gets for another player to place a lower valued die on that action tile.) The possible actions (some of these combined, depending on how the number of players) from which a player can choose are:


    Temple: Send one of your priest to the temple track
    Building: Place a building tile on your player board (if you have enough resources).
    Expanding (across water or land): Place a hut from you player board on one of the island areas on the main board that is adjacent to  an area where you already have build a hut.
    Take a man/woman tile: Take a man/woman tile from the main board, and place it on (on an empty spot) your player board.
    Helper: This action give a lots of possibilities which are explained later
    Fishing: Get 2 Victory Points (VP)

For example, the Take a man action tile. If a player, places a 6 die on the tile, he is allowed to take one of the six man-tiles on the main board. When the player places a 2 die on the action-tile, he could choose from the two most-left man-tiles displayed on the main-board. When he would have placed a 5 die, he could have picked on of the 5 most-left man-tiles. The same basic principle goes on for the rest of the action-tiles. I.e. the Building tile. When a player places a 5 die on it, he can choose one from his available buildings on his player board valued 1 to 5. If he places a 1 die on it, he could only pick the building showing the 1.

The last example shows the most interesting in this mechanism. While all players rolls there die simultaneously, all players know the values of the other player’s dice. By placing a 1 on the building action-tile, the player blocks this tile for other players to use, as they can’t place a lower numbered die on it. But the low number of the placed die, also limits the choice of the current player back to 1 building.  (There are ways to avoid the limits of die-placing using god-cards, but they are explained in the rules)

The ‘Helper’ action gives different options and allows the player to spend the value of the placed die on several different things such as upgrading males or female, moving huts on the player board, and gaining building materials, god cards and offerings tokens.  Upgrading males gives them tattoos (which give them status which determines player order and give VP’s). Upgrading females gives them shelves which they can use to buy jewels (which give VP’s).

It surprised me that this mechanism of using dice in fact works really well. It doesn’t matter that much how your roll, as every die can be used good. Of course the ideal roll of the dice would be something like 6-4-1 every round, but that won’t happen that much. The mechanism creates also a lot of tension, because a player has to think if he uses a high or a low die. Use a low die, to block another player, or use a high die to get maximum effort of the die. And the rare moment, you really can’t use a die, you can always place it on the fishing tile, as the number of a die, doesn’t matter on that tile, and score 2 VP’s that way. I thought before we played the game for the first time, that the action would be used a lot, but it isn’t that interesting, as it only scores  2 VP’s.

Phase 2: Use 1 man tile and 1 woman tile

After the players have finished all the actions that comes with their dice, the game progresses into phase 2. Each player can activate 1 man tile and 1 woman tile. If a player doesn’t have one of these, the player can’t activate the tile, and the player won’t get any of the benefits. The power of the man and woman tiles doesn’t seem to be that big, but you have to keep in mind that they are cumulative. That means, if you have two man-tiles, that would make it possible to build a 1-3 tile, you can add these two tile together, and you can build a 1-6 tile. Just as the actions in phase 1, these man and woman tiles seems pretty balanced, and not getting one of each kind in the very first round, seems as a missed opportunity to me, as you just miss two action in the game. And when you keep in mind, that you will only get 30 actions maximum in this game, missing 1 action, is missing at least 3% of the possibilities to maximize your score. And 3% in a Feld game is a lot.

Phase 3: Resolve the end of round

At the end of the round, the players have to resolve 4 things:

    Resolve the status track. The status track determines the player order (which changes immediately) and give VP’s
    Resolve the priest track. The priest gives VP points.
    Buy jewelry using collected shells. Jewelry give you VP’s at the end of the game, and can help you to complete an objective
    Complete an objective, or discard one, if none can be completed. After completing an objective, get a new objective.

The objectives are a very nice part of the game. Each player will have 3 objectives at the bottom of their personal player board. The objectives can be anything like ‘have 3 different woman’, ‘have 3 of the same jewels’ or ‘have built a house on 4 different types of islands’. When a player completes an objective he gains 6 VP’s. When he uses a God-card to complete it, he will get 4 VP’s. If he can’t complete an objective, he has to discard one, and he will get 0 VP’s. During the complete game, a player can complete 9 objectives, with which he can score 54 VP’s. In our first game, we ended with 150 and 126 VP’s, so 54 is a huge amount of VP’s if you can manage to score the game.

End of the game scoring

When all players have played 6 rounds, they will still have 3 objectives at the bottom of their personal player board (they’ll have to pick a new objective at the end of round 6). They get the chance to complete these 3 objectives, and score points for them. This way a player can complete 9 objectives in the complete game. After all player has completed (or not) their objectives, it’s time for final scoring. A player will get VP’s for:


    Unused God-tiles
    Fish-tiles in the area they have built
    Jewelry-tiles they have bought

There are 9 different way to score 6 VP’s at the end. For example for building all houses, for completing all objectives and so on.

This is Bora Bora in a nutshell. As usual in a Feld game, there is a lot going on, and there are a lot of small rules. Surprisingly it’s not a difficult game at all. Okay, explaining the game may take a while, but once you’re playing, all the little mechanics works very smoothly together. The biggest minor in the game, is the fact that the 9 different ways to score 6 VP’s at the end of the game, are too low, compared what you will have to do to gain the 6 points. For example, when you’ve managed to complete all 9 objectives, you’ve scored 54 VP’s. To manage to complete all 9 things, you will have to let other things pass. After all the effort, you get 6 VP’s. Also some of the ‘6 VP’s bonuses’ are very dependent on each-other. To have 12 people in your tribe, you will have to build 12 houses. The dice-mechanic works very nice, and provides the interaction in this game. Jusrt like other Feld-games, the theme is pasted on, and the game relies on game-mechanics. I think I don’t have to say that this is a family-game or a starters-game. This is a game for experienced players.

When playing, one have to keep one thing in mind. In Trajan you can do everything a bit, and win the game. In contrast to Bora Bora. This game is not forgiving. You’ll hate it, or you’ll love it.

And we love it… 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
